والتضمنية مهجورة بعضا والمطابقية معتبرة مطلقا كتعريف زيد بأنه الماشي الناطق ، فان للماشي يلزمه الحيوان كما لا يخفى فيدل على بعض ماهيته بالالتزام واستعمال اللفظ المشترك فيه الذى لا يصح إرادة كل واحد من معانيه كالعلم في تعريف فن المناظرة بأنه علم يعرف به صحيح الدفع وفاسده أو استعمال اللفظ المجاز بدون الفرينة قيد للأخيرين ، ومن جمله قيدا للأربعة فقد أخطأ كمالا يخفى على من له تأمل صادق . اعلم أن المجاز عند أهل الأصول لفظ استعمل في غير ما وضع له في اصطلاح به التخاطب العلاقة بينهما فيعم الكنانة البيانية ، وهي لفظ أريد به لازم معناه مع جواز إرادته . والمجاز البياني لكون معرفة معنى النار سهلا من معرفة معنى النفس لأن معنى النار من المحسوسات دون معنى النفس ومن شرائط صحة التعريف كونه أجلى من المعرف على أنه لا مجال هنالمنع الكبرى بل يمنع الصغرى مستندا بتحرير التعريف أو المعرف بحيث يظهر به كون التعريف أجلى من المعرف وأما استعمال الألفظ الغريبة الغير المعتادة وإرادة المدلول الإلتزامي إن قلت إرادة للدلول الإلتزامي إن كانت قصدية فهي الحقيقة فى استعمال المجازوإن كانت تبعية فما الفائدة في إرادته إذ هو ليس بمدخل ولا مخرج، ولا إيضاح فى الإرادة التبعية كما لا يخفى . قلت يختار أنه مراد تبعا للاطلاق على الذاتى لكن لما كانت مهجورة فى التعريف مطلقا أو في الحد مطلقا أو في النام مطلقا أو في الحد التام أزالت حسنه فاعرف واستعمال اللفظ للمشترك الذى لا يصح إرادة كل من معانيه أو المجاز الأصولي ، وهو لفظ استعمل في غير ما وضع له في اصطلاح به التخاطب العلاقة بينهما فيهم الكناية البيانية ، وهو لفظ أريد به لازم معناه مع جواز إرادته . وأما المجاز البياني: فهو لفظ استعمل في غير ما وضع له في اصطلاح به التخاطب العلاقة بينهما بقرينة صارفة عما وضع له فهذا لايعم البكتابة لكون القرينة جزءا منه ومباينة الجنسها فاعرف بدون القرينة وهو لفظ استعمل في غير ما وضع له في اصطلاح به التخاطب العلاقة مع قرينة ما نعة عن إرادته والقرينة المطلقة ما يفسح عن المراد لا بالوضع، وهى إما مانعة أو معينة : أما المانعة فهي ما يمتنع عنده إرادة للعنى الحقيقى حما أو عقلا أو شرعا أو عادة ، وأما المعينة فهى ما يعين المراد ويوضحه ، فالمجاز لا بد له من قرينتين مانعة ومعينة ، والكناية لا بد لها أيضا من قرينتين قرينة للانتقال ، وقرينة معينة للمراد والمشترك لا بد له من قرينة معينة له ، وأما الحقيقة فلا يلزم أن يكون لها قرينة الواضحة المعينة للمراد صفة للقرينة ، واحترز به عن القرينة المانعة للمجاز لأنها داخلة فى مفهوم المجاز فلا يصبح المجاز بدونها ولا يصح التعريف المشتمل للمجاز بدونها فهو أى كل واحد من هذه الأمور يذهب من الإذهاب حسن التعريف لا صحته إذ السامع يحتاج إلى الاستفسار في هذه الأمور وهو غير مناسب لمقام التعريف ، ولأن لكل معنى لوازم متعددة فلا يتعين اللازم الذي أريد في المدلول الالتزامى، وعدم تعين المراد في المشترك تتزاحم معانيه وعدم تعينه في المجاز ولعدم علمه بالمدلول في الغريب ، وكذا يجب الاحتراز عن استعمال اللفظ الغير العربي واللفظ المستدرك واللفظ الضعيف بناء وإعرابا وعن التخصيص بلا مخصص ، وينبغي أن يعلم أن هذه الأمور إنما تذهب حسن التعريف لا صحته في التعريف الحقيقى ، وأما فى التعريف اللفظى ، فهو يذهب صحته قطعا وتصوير النقض بهذه الأمور أن يقال إن هذا التعريف مثلا مشتمل على اللفظ الغريب أو على المدلول الالتزامى أو على اللفظ المشترك بدون قرينة معينة أو على المجاز بدونها ، وكل ما كان كذلك ، فهو ليس بجيد . وأما الجواب من صاحب التعريف فيعرف بالمقايسة فتأمل واستخرج والظاهر أن النقض بأحد هذه الأمور نقض إجمالى مجازى لأن النقض إبطال الدليل أو التعريف أو التقسم ولا إبطال هنا فلا يكون حقيقيا إذا كان للعنى للقصود من هذه الألفاظ أحلى من للعرف وأما إذا لم يكن أجلى فيعترض على التعريف ببطلانه لا بانتفاء حسنه فقط . اعلم أن استعمال أحد هذه الأمور في التعريف إنما يذهب حسن التعريف لاصحته إذا كان التعريف جامعا ومانها وخاليا عن المحالات ، وكان أحلى من المعرف تقوله إذا كان للعنى المقصود أحلى من المعرف الواضحة المعينة للمراد قيد للآخرين إذ لا معنى للقرينة في الألفاظ الغربة . وأما إرادة المدلول الالتزامي فالمسمى قرينة معينة لها فلا يتصور أنفكاكه فتأمل فهو أي استعمال كل من المذكورات وكذا استعمال اللفظ الوحشى القير العذب واللفظ المستدرك واللفظ الضعيف بناؤه أو إعرابه غلط لفظى يذهب حسن التعريف لا صحته إذ السامع يحتاج إلى الاستفادة من المراد وهو غير مناسب المقام التعريف وذا لتعدد المدلول وعدم تعين المراد في المشترك ولعدم تعينه في المجازو لعدم علمه بالمدلول أصلا في الغريب وأما إزالة الإرادة فلكونها مهجورة في التعريفات عند المحققين وأما القرينة الصارفة فهى أعم من المعينة وشرطية الأخص تستلزم شرطية الأعم مع أنها داخلة في مفهوم المجاز البياني وأما إذا كان التعريف لفظيا فيذهب بذلك الأربعة المذكورة صحته وبالأربعة التي ذكرناها حسنه قتبصر، وقوله إذا كان المعنى المقصود أجلى من المعرف لاحاجة إليه إذ قيد الحيثية معتبر في الجميع وإلا لاحتيج إلى أن يقال ولم يستلزم لفسادها ، وتقريره أن هذا التعريف مشتمل على لفظ كنا حسن والمنوع الجارية فى هذا الدليل وأسانيدها غير خفية على من له حظ فى العربية
And it is an indication of the proof applying the example to the like, and it is in the final analysis a proof of the mentioned minor premise. Its representation is that it is said: This definition is not clearer than the definiendum because it is a definition by the soul, and the soul is more hidden than it. And everything that is like that—not clearer than the definiendum—is not clearer. (And among the conditions of a sound definition is that it be) meaning the definition (clearer than the definiendum). It may be related to his saying 'it may refute the definition' and may be related to his saying 'and the soul is more hidden'. In both cases it is an indication of the implied major premise, that it is said: Everything that is not clearer than the definiendum is invalid because among the conditions of the correctness of the definition is etc. And in it there is a reminder that there is no room to deny the major premise, rather the minor premise is denied based on reformulating the definition or the definiendum such that the definition appears clearer. And he indicated by the partitive 'min' and the nature of plurality the abundance of conditions, and they are three matters: equality of the definiens to the definiendum which is inclusion and exclusion, its being free of impossibilities, and its being clearer than the definiendum. And leaving the duties of the definer, which are denial of the minor premise, objection, and investigative refutation, and changing the definition wholly or partially, is referral to comparison and an indication of the rarity of this refutation and its answer. And as for using strange words in the definition, such as saying 'fire is an element above elements'; and usage is mentioning the word and intending its meaning absolutely, and strangeness is the word being unfamiliar, not apparent in meaning, and not customary in usage, like 'taka ka'anam wa fir naqa'u'. And intending the implicative meaning from the definition or from part of its parts, for implicative indication is abandoned absolutely, and inclusive indication is partially abandoned, and conformative indication is considered absolutely, like defining Zayd as 'the walking speaker', for 'walking' necessitates 'animal' as is clear, so it indicates part of his essence by implication. And using the ambiguous word where it is not valid to intend each of its meanings, like 'knowledge' in defining the art of disputation as 'knowledge by which sound and unsound argument is known'. Or using metaphorical word without a qarina, which is a condition for the last two, and whoever includes it as a condition for all four has erred, as is not hidden to one who has sincere reflection. Know that majaz according to the uṣūliyyūn is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship between them, and it includes rhetorical kināyah, which is a word by which its necessary meaning is intended while it is permissible to intend its original meaning. And the figurative majaz because knowing the meaning of fire is easier than knowing the meaning of the soul, because the meaning of fire is among sensibles while the meaning of the soul is not. And from the conditions of the correctness of the definition is that it be clearer than the definiendum, on the basis that there is no room to deny the major premise, rather the minor premise is denied based on reformulating the definition or the definiendum such that the definition appears clearer than the definiendum. And as for using strange words that are unusual and intending the implicative meaning: If you say, the intending of the implicative meaning, if it is intentional, then it is actually using majaz; if it is subsidiary, what benefit is there in intending it since it is neither an entry nor an exit, and there is no clarification in subsidiary intending as is clear. I say: It is chosen that it is intended subsidiary to the absolute statement about the essential, but since it is abandoned in definition absolutely, or in the definition (ḥadd) absolutely, or in the nam (?) absolutely, or in the complete definition, it removes its beauty, so recognize. And using the ambiguous word that is not valid to intend each of its meanings, or the uṣūlī majaz, which is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship between them, and it includes rhetorical kināyah, which is a word by which its necessary meaning is intended while it is permissible to intend its original meaning. As for figurative majaz: it is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship between them with a qarina that turns away from what it was coined for; so this does not include kināyah because the qarina is part of it and its genus is different, so recognize. Without the qarina, it is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship with a qarina that prevents its intended meaning. And the absolute qarina is what reveals the intended meaning not by coinage, and it is either preventing or specifying. As for the preventing, it is that by which intending the literal meaning is prevented, whether by sense, reason, law, or custom. As for the specifying, it is that which specifies the intended meaning and clarifies it. Thus majaz necessarily has two qarinas: a preventing and a specifying. Kināyah also necessarily has two qarinas: a qarina for transfer and a specifying qarina for the intended meaning. The ambiguous word necessarily has a specifying qarina for it. As for the literal, it is not necessary that it have a clarifying specifying qarina for the intended meaning, which is a qualification of the qarina, and he guards thereby against the preventing qarina for majaz because it is included in the concept of majaz, so majaz is not valid without it. And the definition that includes majaz is not correct without it. So each one of these matters removes the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, because the hearer needs to ask for clarification in these matters, and that is not appropriate for the context of definition; and because every meaning has multiple necessary concomitants, so the necessary that is intended in the implicative meaning is not determined; and lack of determination of the intended in the ambiguous word results in its meanings conflicting; and lack of determination of the intended in majaz; and due to lack of knowledge of the meaning at all in the strange word. Likewise, one must avoid using non-Arabic words, and the obsolete word, and the weak word in construction and parsing, and specification without a specifier. And it should be known that these matters remove the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, in the real definition. As for the verbal definition, it removes its correctness absolutely. And the depiction of the criticism (naqd) by these matters is that it is said: This definition, for example, includes a strange word, or an implicative meaning, or an ambiguous word without a specifying qarina, or a majaz without it. And everything that is like that is not good. As for the answer from the definer, it is known by comparison, so reflect and extract. And it appears that the criticism by these matters is a summary figurative criticism because criticism is invalidation of the proof, definition, or division, and there is no invalidation here, so it is not real. If the intended meaning of these words is clearer than the definiendum, then it is a criticism of the definition as invalid, not just lack of beauty. Know that using one of these matters in the definition removes the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, if the definition is inclusive and exclusive and free of impossibilities and clearer than the definiendum. You say: if the intended meaning is clearer than the definiendum, the clarifying specifying qarina is a condition for the others, for there is no meaning to the qarina in the strange words. As for intending the implicative meaning, the one who specifies the qarina for it cannot be separated, so reflect. So each one of the mentioned usages, and likewise using an unfamiliar word, and the obsolete word, and the weak word in construction or parsing, a verbal error, removes the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, because the hearer needs to ascertain the intended meaning, and that is not appropriate for the context of definition, and due to multiplicity of meanings and lack of determination of the intended in the ambiguous word, and lack of determination in the majaz, and lack of knowledge of the meaning at all in the strange word. As for removing the intended meaning, it is because it is abandoned in definitions among the verifiers. As for the turning qarina, it is more general than the specifying one, and the conditionality of the specific entails the conditionality of the general, with it being included in the concept of figurative majaz. And if the definition is verbal, then the four mentioned remove its correctness, and the four we mentioned remove its beauty, so be discerning. And his statement: 'if the intended meaning is clearer than the definiendum' is unnecessary, for the condition of respect is considered in all, otherwise it would need to be said that it does not entail its invalidity. And its explanation: this definition includes a word that is like beautiful, and the prevalent prohibitions in this proof and their supports are not hidden to one who has a portion in Arabic.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conditions of a sound definition (ta'rīf). It begins by discussing a proof that a definition must be clearer (ajlā) than the definiendum. The author explains that if a definition defines something by itself (al-nafs), it is not clearer, and thus invalid. The proof uses a conditional syllogism: if a definition is not clearer than the definiendum, it is not valid. The major premise is that a valid definition must be clearer. The minor premise is that this definition is not clearer. The author notes that the opponent cannot deny the major premise but may deny the minor by showing the definition is actually clearer. He then lists three general conditions for a definition: equality (musāwāt) in inclusion and exclusion, freedom from impossibilities, and being clearer than the definiendum. He also mentions the duties of the definer in responding to objections. Then the passage turns to four specific usages that spoil the beauty (ḥusn) of a definition, though not necessarily its correctness: 1) strange words (alfāẓ gharība), 2) implicative meaning (dalāla iltizāmiyya), 3) ambiguous words (al-lafẓ al-mushtarak), and 4) metaphorical usage (majāz) without a clarifying qarīna. For each, examples are given. Strange words are those unfamiliar and not commonly understood, like the nonsense example 'tak ka' anam wa fir naqa'u'. Implicative meaning refers to a definition that indirectly indicates a part of the essence, like defining Zayd as 'the walking speaker' which implies 'animal' by necessity; such indication is abandoned in definitions. Ambiguous words are those with multiple meanings that cannot all be intended simultaneously, like 'knowledge' in a definition of dialectic. Metaphorical usage without a qarīna is also problematic. The author then delves into a detailed discussion of types of majāz (uṣūlī vs. bayānī) and qarīna (preventing vs. specifying). He explains that kināyah (indirect suggestion) is included in uṣūlī majāz but not in bayānī majāz. A proper definition must avoid these pitfalls because they confuse the hearer and defeat the purpose of clarification. The passage also distinguishes between real definitions (ḥaqīqī) and verbal definitions (lafẓī). In real definitions, these issues only affect beauty; in verbal definitions, they affect correctness. Finally, it discusses how to formulate an objection (naqḍ) based on these issues and how the definer might respond, emphasizing that such a criticism is not a true invalidation but a judgment of lack of beauty, unless the definition is not clearer.

Why is defining Zayd as 'the walking speaker' considered problematic?
A) It uses a strange word.
B) It is a metaphorical expression.
C) It relies on implicative indication.

Key Takeaway
These four usages make a definition unclear and ruin its beauty, even if it is logically correct.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the four usages is present in the following definition: 'Fiqh is the knowledge of the practical shar'i rulings from their detailed evidence.' Is it clear or does it contain any of the four usages?
?
Rewrite the definition of 'iman' that someone gave as 'the state of the heart by which one enters paradise' to avoid implicative meaning.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
8 min
Types of Majaz and Qarina in Definitions
Author explains types of majaz and qarina, and their effect on definitions.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between uṣūlī majaz (including kinayah) and bayani majaz, identify the necessary qarinas for each, and explain how these affect the beauty and correctness of real vs. verbal definitions.

Core Idea
Metaphor (majaz) and ambiguity require qarinas; otherwise definitions lose clarity and sometimes validity.

The author delves into the technical details of majaz (metaphorical usage) and qarina (contextual indicator). He distinguishes between two types of majaz: uṣūlī (which includes kinayah) and bayānī (figurative). For each, a qarina is required, and he explains the roles of preventing and specifying qarinas. This discussion is crucial for understanding when a definition that uses metaphor or kinayah is acceptable.

Guided Path
1
Differentiate the two types of majaz
Uṣūlī majaz is a word used in other than its original meaning with a relationship, and it includes kinayah (where the necessary meaning is intended but original is possible). Bayānī majaz is similar but requires a qarina that turns away from the original meaning; kinayah is not included.
The distinction depends on whether the qarina is part of the concept.
2
Understand the roles of qarinas
A preventing qarina (mani'a) prevents the literal meaning; a specifying qarina (mu'ayyina) specifies the intended meaning. Majaz needs both; kinayah needs a transfer qarina and a specifying qarina; ambiguous words need a specifying qarina only; literal words need none.
3
Apply to real vs. verbal definitions
In real definitions, these usages only spoil beauty; in verbal definitions (where the goal is to clarify the meaning of a word), they actually invalidate the definition because the definition must be equally clear to the word being defined.

وأما الجواب من صاحب التعريف فيعرف بالمقايسة فتأمل واستخرج والظاهر أن النقض بأحد هذه الأمور نقض إجمالى مجازى لأن النقض إبطال الدليل أو التعريف أو التقسم ولا إبطال هنا فلا يكون حقيقيا إذا كان للعنى And it is an indication of the proof applying the example to the like, and it is in the final analysis a proof of the mentioned minor premise. Its representation is that it is said: This definition is not clearer than the definiendum because it is a definition by the soul, and the soul is more hidden than it. And everything that is like that—not clearer than the definiendum—is not clearer. (And among the conditions of a sound definition is that it be) meaning the definition (clearer than the definiendum). It may be related to his saying 'it may refute the definition' and may be related to his saying 'and the soul is more hidden'. In both cases it is an indication of the implied major premise, that it is said: Everything that is not clearer than the definiendum is invalid because among the conditions of the correctness of the definition is etc. And in it there is a reminder that there is no room to deny the major premise, rather the minor premise is denied based on reformulating the definition or the definiendum such that the definition appears clearer. And he indicated by the partitive 'min' and the nature of plurality the abundance of conditions, and they are three matters: equality of the definiens to the definiendum which is inclusion and exclusion, its being free of impossibilities, and its being clearer than the definiendum. And leaving the duties of the definer, which are denial of the minor premise, objection, and investigative refutation, and changing the definition wholly or partially, is referral to comparison and an indication of the rarity of this refutation and its answer. And as for using strange words in the definition, such as saying 'fire is an element above elements'; and usage is mentioning the word and intending its meaning absolutely, and strangeness is the word being unfamiliar, not apparent in meaning, and not customary in usage, like 'taka ka'anam wa fir naqa'u'. And intending the implicative meaning from the definition or from part of its parts, for implicative indication is abandoned absolutely, and inclusive indication is partially abandoned, and conformative indication is considered absolutely, like defining Zayd as 'the walking speaker', for 'walking' necessitates 'animal' as is clear, so it indicates part of his essence by implication. And using the ambiguous word where it is not valid to intend each of its meanings, like 'knowledge' in defining the art of disputation as 'knowledge by which sound and unsound argument is known'. Or using metaphorical word without a qarina, which is a condition for the last two, and whoever includes it as a condition for all four has erred, as is not hidden to one who has sincere reflection. Know that majaz according to the uṣūliyyūn is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship between them, and it includes rhetorical kināyah, which is a word by which its necessary meaning is intended while it is permissible to intend its original meaning. And the figurative majaz because knowing the meaning of fire is easier than knowing the meaning of the soul, because the meaning of fire is among sensibles while the meaning of the soul is not. And from the conditions of the correctness of the definition is that it be clearer than the definiendum, on the basis that there is no room to deny the major premise, rather the minor premise is denied based on reformulating the definition or the definiendum such that the definition appears clearer than the definiendum. And as for using strange words that are unusual and intending the implicative meaning: If you say, the intending of the implicative meaning, if it is intentional, then it is actually using majaz; if it is subsidiary, what benefit is there in intending it since it is neither an entry nor an exit, and there is no clarification in subsidiary intending as is clear. I say: It is chosen that it is intended subsidiary to the absolute statement about the essential, but since it is abandoned in definition absolutely, or in the definition (ḥadd) absolutely, or in the nam (?) absolutely, or in the complete definition, it removes its beauty, so recognize. And using the ambiguous word that is not valid to intend each of its meanings, or the uṣūlī majaz, which is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship between them, and it includes rhetorical kināyah, which is a word by which its necessary meaning is intended while it is permissible to intend its original meaning. As for figurative majaz: it is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship between them with a qarina that turns away from what it was coined for; so this does not include kināyah because the qarina is part of it and its genus is different, so recognize. Without the qarina, it is a word used in other than what it was coined for in the convention of discourse with a relationship with a qarina that prevents its intended meaning. And the absolute qarina is what reveals the intended meaning not by coinage, and it is either preventing or specifying. As for the preventing, it is that by which intending the literal meaning is prevented, whether by sense, reason, law, or custom. As for the specifying, it is that which specifies the intended meaning and clarifies it. Thus majaz necessarily has two qarinas: a preventing and a specifying. Kināyah also necessarily has two qarinas: a qarina for transfer and a specifying qarina for the intended meaning. The ambiguous word necessarily has a specifying qarina for it. As for the literal, it is not necessary that it have a clarifying specifying qarina for the intended meaning, which is a qualification of the qarina, and he guards thereby against the preventing qarina for majaz because it is included in the concept of majaz, so majaz is not valid without it. And the definition that includes majaz is not correct without it. So each one of these matters removes the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, because the hearer needs to ask for clarification in these matters, and that is not appropriate for the context of definition; and because every meaning has multiple necessary concomitants, so the necessary that is intended in the implicative meaning is not determined; and lack of determination of the intended in the ambiguous word results in its meanings conflicting; and lack of determination of the intended in majaz; and due to lack of knowledge of the meaning at all in the strange word. Likewise, one must avoid using non-Arabic words, and the obsolete word, and the weak word in construction and parsing, and specification without a specifier. And it should be known that these matters remove the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, in the real definition. As for the verbal definition, it removes its correctness absolutely. And the depiction of the criticism (naqd) by these matters is that it is said: This definition, for example, includes a strange word, or an implicative meaning, or an ambiguous word without a specifying qarina, or a majaz without it. And everything that is like that is not good. As for the answer from the definer, it is known by comparison, so reflect and extract. And it appears that the criticism by these matters is a summary figurative criticism because criticism is invalidation of the proof, definition, or division, and there is no invalidation here, so it is not real. If the intended meaning of these words is clearer than the definiendum, then it is a criticism of the definition as invalid, not just lack of beauty. Know that using one of these matters in the definition removes the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, if the definition is inclusive and exclusive and free of impossibilities and clearer than the definiendum. You say: if the intended meaning is clearer than the definiendum, the clarifying specifying qarina is a condition for the others, for there is no meaning to the qarina in the strange words. As for intending the implicative meaning, the one who specifies the qarina for it cannot be separated, so reflect. So each one of the mentioned usages, and likewise using an unfamiliar word, and the obsolete word, and the weak word in construction or parsing, a verbal error, removes the beauty of the definition, not its correctness, because the hearer needs to ascertain the intended meaning, and that is not appropriate for the context of definition, and due to multiplicity of meanings and lack of determination of the intended in the ambiguous word, and lack of determination in the majaz, and lack of knowledge of the meaning at all in the strange word. As for removing the intended meaning, it is because it is abandoned in definitions among the verifiers. As for the turning qarina, it is more general than the specifying one, and the conditionality of the specific entails the conditionality of the general, with it being included in the concept of figurative majaz. And if the definition is verbal, then the four mentioned remove its correctness, and the four we mentioned remove its beauty, so be discerning. And his statement: 'if the intended meaning is clearer than the definiendum' is unnecessary, for the condition of respect is considered in all, otherwise it would need to be said that it does not entail its invalidity. And its explanation: this definition includes a word that is like beautiful, and the prevalent prohibitions in this proof and their supports are not hidden to one who has a portion in Arabic.
Source pages p. 26

Commentary
This passage is an expansion on the conditions of a sound definition (ta'rīf). It begins by discussing a proof that a definition must be clearer (ajlā) than the definiendum. The author explains that if a definition defines something by itself (al-nafs), it is not clearer, and thus invalid. The proof uses a conditional syllogism: if a definition is not clearer than the definiendum, it is not valid. The major premise is that a valid definition must be clearer. The minor premise is that this definition is not clearer. The author notes that the opponent cannot deny the major premise but may deny the minor by showing the definition is actually clearer. He then lists three general conditions for a definition: equality (musāwāt) in inclusion and exclusion, freedom from impossibilities, and being clearer than the definiendum. He also mentions the duties of the definer in responding to objections. Then the passage turns to four specific usages that spoil the beauty (ḥusn) of a definition, though not necessarily its correctness: 1) strange words (alfāẓ gharība), 2) implicative meaning (dalāla iltizāmiyya), 3) ambiguous words (al-lafẓ al-mushtarak), and 4) metaphorical usage (majāz) without a clarifying qarīna. For each, examples are given. Strange words are those unfamiliar and not commonly understood, like the nonsense example 'tak ka' anam wa fir naqa'u'. Implicative meaning refers to a definition that indirectly indicates a part of the essence, like defining Zayd as 'the walking speaker' which implies 'animal' by necessity; such indication is abandoned in definitions. Ambiguous words are those with multiple meanings that cannot all be intended simultaneously, like 'knowledge' in a definition of dialectic. Metaphorical usage without a qarīna is also problematic. The author then delves into a detailed discussion of types of majāz (uṣūlī vs. bayānī) and qarīna (preventing vs. specifying). He explains that kināyah (indirect suggestion) is included in uṣūlī majāz but not in bayānī majāz. A proper definition must avoid these pitfalls because they confuse the hearer and defeat the purpose of clarification. The passage also distinguishes between real definitions (ḥaqīqī) and verbal definitions (lafẓī). In real definitions, these issues only affect beauty; in verbal definitions, they affect correctness. Finally, it discusses how to formulate an objection (naqḍ) based on these issues and how the definer might respond, emphasizing that such a criticism is not a true invalidation but a judgment of lack of beauty, unless the definition is not clearer.

Goodness vs. Correctness in Definitions: The Role of Contextual Indicators
Author explains the necessity of qarinas for majaz, kinaya, mushtarak and their effect on definition's goodness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between correctness (ṣiḥḥa) and goodness (ḥusn) of a definition, and identify when a definition's use of ambiguous language affects its validity versus its rhetorical quality.

Core Idea
A definition can be logically correct yet rhetorically poor.

In logic, the goodness of a definition (ḥusn al-ta'rīf) is not the same as its logical correctness (ṣiḥḥa). The author of the Waldiyya commentary argues that using metaphorical, allusive, ambiguous, or unfamiliar words in a definition does not necessarily invalidate the definition logically, but it does reduce its quality. Understanding this distinction is crucial for producing definitions that are both sound and clear.

Guided Path
1
Identify the four types of problematic expressions
Look at the passage: it mentions majāz (metaphor), kināya (allusion), mushtarak (ambiguous word), and gharīb (unfamiliar word). Each has specific requirements for contextual indicators.
2
Determine the type of contextual indicator each requires
Metaphor and allusion require two indicators (preventing and specifying); ambiguous word requires only a specifying indicator; unfamiliar word requires no indicator but is inherently unclear.
The qarīna is either māni'a or mu'ayyina.
3
Distinguish between real and verbal definitions
In a real definition (ḥadd ḥaqīqī), these expressions only affect goodness, not correctness. In a verbal definition (ḥadd lafẓī), they affect correctness absolutely.
The author states: 'in a real definition... these matters only remove the goodness... in a verbal definition, it removes its correctness absolutely.'
4
Apply the clarity condition
The definition must be clearer (ajlā) than the definiendum. If not, the definition is invalid, not just poorly worded.
The passage discusses the condition 'if the intended meaning is clearer than the definiendum'.

Source pages p. 27

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the requirements for a good definition, expanding on the matn's points about avoiding ambiguous or metaphorical language. The author begins by defining a metaphorical term (majaz) as a word used for other than its original coinage, requiring a relationship and two contextual indicators (qarina): a preventing indicator (mani'a) that prevents the literal meaning, and a specifying indicator (mu'ayyina) that specifies the intended meaning. Similarly, allusion (kinaya) requires two indicators: one for transfer and one for specification; an ambiguous word (mushtarak) requires a specifying indicator; only literal terms (haqiqa) may not require an indicator. The key point is that using such ambiguous elements in a definition only harms its goodness (husn) — i.e., its clarity and elegance — not its correctness (sihha), i.e., its logical validity. The author then lists other things to avoid: unfamiliar words, obsolete words, weak morphology/syntax, and arbitrary specification. He emphasizes that in a real definition (hadd haqiqi), these only affect goodness; but in a verbal definition (hadd lafzi), they affect correctness. The passage then addresses an objection about the classification of refutations: that pointing out these flaws is a valid refutation (naqd) even though it does not invalidate the definition; the author replies that such refutation is metaphorical, not real, because real refutation invalidates the definition. Finally, he discusses the condition that the definition must be clearer (ajla) than the definiendum; if not, the definition is not just poorly worded but actually invalid. The commentary illustrates the importance of distinguishing between a definition's logical soundness and its rhetorical quality in the Islamic logical tradition.

Distinguishing Correctness from Goodness in Definition Critique
Author defends completeness of critique division by separating correctness from goodness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a critique targeting logical correctness from a critique targeting stylistic goodness, and explain why the standard four-part division of critique remains complete despite stylistic objections.

Core Idea
Critique of definition targets logical correctness, not stylistic elegance—the two must never be confused.

In this passage, the author defends a classification of types of critique against an objection that it is incomplete. The objector argues that using strange or unclear words in a definition constitutes a critique that falls outside the four listed types. The author's response introduces a crucial distinction: there is a difference between a definition's logical correctness (sihha) and its goodness or elegance (husn). The four-part division targets only critiques of correctness, not stylistic complaints. Understanding this distinction is essential for precise logical analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objector's claim
The objector argues that the division of critique into four types (lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, entailing impossibility, not being clearer) is incomplete because using strange words is a critique that is not included.
The objector assumes all critiques must fit the division.
2
Locate the author's key distinction
The author replies that using strange words affects only the goodness (husn) of the definition, not its correctness (sihha). The term 'critique' here is used metaphorically.
This is the central move: separating logical validity from stylistic quality.
3
Understand why the division remains complete
Since the division specifically addresses critiques of correctness, stylistic flaws are not part of the divided whole (the set of proper critiques). Thus, the division is fully encompassing.
The objector's critique is not a genuine counterexample.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example
Consider a definition of 'wine' as 'an intoxicating beverage' vs. using an obscure synonym. The first is correct and good; the second is correct but not good. Only the first kind of defect matters for logical correctness.
This shows the practical use of the distinction.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما استعمال الألفاظ الغريبة الخ جواب سؤال على حصر التقسيم لأن التقسيم فى قوة أن يقال إن النقض على التعريف إما نقض عليه بعدم الجمع أو بعدم المنع أو باستلزامه المحال أو يعدم كونه أجلى من المعرف ، وذلك بأن يقال : هذا التقسيم غير حاصر لأقسامه لخروج النقض بأحد هذه الأمور عن التقسيم مع دخوله في المقسم فيكون فاسدا. وأجابة عنه بقوله : وأما استعمال الألفاظ الغريبة الخ . وحاصله أنا لا نسلم أن النقض بأحد هذه الأمور داخل في المقسم لأن كل واحد من هذه الأمور يذهب حسن التعريف لا صحته فلا يكون النقض بأحد هذه الأمور داخلا في المقسم لأن إطلاق النقض عليه مجاز كما مر.
And as for the use of strange words, etc., it is an answer to a question about the completeness of the division, because the division effectively states that critique of a definition is either by lack of comprehensiveness, or by lack of exclusiveness, or by entailing impossibility, or by lacking clearness than the definiendum. So one might say: This division does not encompass its parts, because critique by one of these matters is excluded from the division while it is included in the divided whole, so the division is invalid. And his answer to it is by saying: 'As for the use of strange words, etc.' And its summary is that we do not concede that critique by one of these matters is included in the divided whole, because each of these matters removes the goodness of the definition, not its correctness; so critique by one of these matters is not included in the divided whole, because applying the term 'critique' to it is metaphorical, as has passed.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical exchange defending the completeness of a classification of types of critique against a potential objection. The objection claimed that the division of critique into four types — by lack of comprehensiveness (jami'), lack of exclusiveness (mani'), entailing absurdity, or not being clearer than the definiendum — is incomplete because using strange words to define would be a critique that falls outside these four. The author responds that the division is indeed complete but the objector has misunderstood the nature of critique using strange words. Such critique does not affect the definition's correctness (sihha) but only its goodness (husn). In the Islamic logical tradition, a definition must be both correct (possessing all necessary conditions) and good (being clear, elegant, etc.). Since the division of critique is concerned with correctness, issues of style or clarity are not considered proper critiques in the technical sense. Thus, the word 'critique' when applied to such cases is used metaphorically. This distinction is crucial: a flawed definition may still be correct if it meets the essential conditions, even if its expression is not ideal. For example, defining 'wine' as 'an intoxicating beverage' is correct even if one uses obscure synonyms; the definition's logical validity stands.

Key Terms
النقض
حسن التعريف
صحة التعريف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
An objector claims that using ambiguous words in a definition invalidates the standard four-part division of critique. How does the author reply?
A) He concedes and amends the division to include stylistic flaws.
B) He argues that stylistic flaws affect correctness, so the division is complete.
C) He distinguishes between goodness and correctness, stating that the division covers only critiques of correctness.
D) He claims that using ambiguous words is not a critique at all.

Key Takeaway
A definition can be logically correct yet stylistically poor; only critiques of correctness challenge the definition's validity.

Definition of naaqid al-ta'rif, muwajjih, and mani'
Author defines the roles of the critic, defender, and refuter in definition debates.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define and distinguish between the roles of naaqid al-ta'rif, muwajjih, and mani' in a definition debate.

Core Idea
In definition debates, the critic must prove his objection; the defender blocks the proof.

In the Islamic logical tradition, definitions are not just stated but debated. This passage establishes the key roles in such debates: the critic (naaqid), who must support his objection with evidence, and the defender (muwajjih or mani'), who repels the objection by denying its premises. Understanding these roles is essential for engaging in rigorous definitional analysis.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three key roles
Locate the terms naaqid al-ta'rif, muwajjih, and mani' in the Arabic. Note that muwajjih and mani' are used synonymously here.
The critic is the one who objects to a definition.
2
Understand the critic's requirement
The author states that the critic must be a 'mustadill' - one who brings a proof. The objection itself is not enough; it must be accompanied by a claim of invalidity and a proof.
The proof can be of four types: lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, entailing corruption, or not being clearer than the definiendum.
3
Understand the defender's role
The defender (muwajjih/mani') repels the objection by preventing (denying) some or all of the premises of the critic's proof.
This is a detailed critique, either bare or with evidence.
4
Apply the definitions
Consider a sample definition debate: if someone defines 'wine' as 'an intoxicating beverage', and a critic objects that it lacks exclusiveness, the critic must provide evidence that there are non-intoxicating beverages called 'wine' or similar. The defender then may deny that premise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: اشتهر بين الطلبة أن ناقض التعريف أى المعترض على التعريف الحقيقى سواء كان حقيقيا أو اسميا ولك أن تعمم التعريف إلى اللفظى والحقيقي مستدل. وما قيل فى هذه العبارة ركاكة إذ الاستدلال مأخود في مفهوم ناقض التعريف بناء على ما سبق من معنى النقض، فالظاهر أن يقول المعترض على التعريف مستدل المدفوع مجمل الناقض على المعترض، نعم لو قيل فيه مساحة لكان له وجه. وموجهه أى دافع ذلك الاعتراض مانع أى ناقض نقضا تفصيلينا مجردا أو مع السند. ومعناه أي معنى قولهم إن ناقض التعريف مستدل وموجهه مانع أن الاعتراض من قبل السائل على التعريف الحقيقى لا يكون بطريق من الطرق إلا ملابسا بطريق إلا دعوى بطلانه أى بطلان ذلك التعريف والاستدلال عطف على دعوى بطلانه ويجوز أن يكون معطوفا على الطريق وهو الاتيان بالدليل على ذلك الدعوى أى دعوى البطلان وتذكير اسم الإشارة باعتبار المذكور فلا يرد عليه أن اسم الإشارة لا يوافق المشار إليه في التذكير والتأنيث بما عرفته متعلق بالاستدلال وهو أعم من أن يكون بعدم الجمع أو بعدم المنع أو باستلزامه خصوص الفساد أو بعدم كونه أجلى هذا معنى ناقض التعريف. وأما معنى موجهه فأشار إليه بقوله وأن الجواب من قبل صاحب التعريف عن ذلك الاعتراض بمنع مقدمات ذلك الدليل كلا أو بعضا مطلقا وقد عرفته أيضا لكن هذا وموجهه مانعا.
Section: It is well-known among students that the critic (naaqid) of a definition, meaning the one who objects to a real definition whether the definition is real or nominal—and you may generalize the definition to verbal and real—is a 'mustadill' (one who provides evidence). What is said about this expression being awkward [is answered]: 'mustadill' is inherent in the concept of naaqid based on the preceding meaning of naqd, so it is apparent that one should say 'the objector to the definition is a mustadill'—the repelled [objection] is summarized as the critic upon the objector. Indeed, if one says 'musahhih' (corrector) there could be a justification. And 'muwajjih' (defender) is the one who repels that objection, i.e., a 'mani'' (preventer), meaning one who criticizes with a detailed critique, bare or with evidence. And its meaning—i.e., the meaning of their statement that the critic of the definition is a mustadill and its defender is a mani'—is that the objection from the questioner against the real definition cannot be in any way except that it is accompanied by a claim of its invalidity, i.e., the invalidity of that definition, and the providing of evidence is conjoined with that claim of invalidity—and it is also permissible that it is conjoined with 'the way'—and that is bringing the proof for that claim, i.e., the claim of invalidity; and the use of the masculine demonstrative pronoun is because of what is mentioned, so it is not objected that the demonstrative pronoun does not agree in gender with what is referred to. 'By what you know' is attached to 'providing evidence', and it is more general than being by lack of comprehensiveness, or by lack of exclusiveness, or by entailing a specific corruption, or by lacking clearness [than the definiendum]. This is the meaning of the critic of the definition. As for the meaning of muwajjih, he indicated it by his saying: 'And the answer from the owner of the definition to that objection is by preventing some or all premises of that proof, absolutely,' and you have known that too, but this and its defender is a mani'.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage defines the key roles in a debate about definitions. It first states the conventional usage among students: the one who criticizes (naaqid) a definition is called a 'mustadill' (one who brings proof), and the one who responds (muwajjih) is called a 'mani'' (one who prevents/refutes). The author clarifies that these terms are based on the nature of critique: a critic must support his objection with evidence, hence he is a 'mustadill'. Similarly, the defender's role is to block the critique by denying premises, hence 'mani''. The passage then expands on the meaning: the critic's objection can only be valid if it includes a claim of invalidity and a proof. The proof can be of four types: lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, entailing corruption, or not being clearer than the definiendum. The defender's answer is to prevent (i.e., reject) some or all premises of that proof. This classification is foundational for understanding the procedure of definition evaluation in Islamic logic. For example, if someone defines 'wine' as 'an intoxicating beverage', a critic might object that it lacks exclusiveness (since non-intoxicating beverages can be made from dates). The critic then must provide evidence, and the defender may prevent a premise in that evidence.

Key Terms
ناقض التعريف
مستدل
مانع
موجهه

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) He must provide a proof for the invalidity of the definition.
C) He must accept the definition as it stands.
D) He must remain silent after the objection.

Key Takeaway
In definition debates, the critic must support his objection with a proof, and the defender repels the objection by denying premises.

Classifying Definitions as Hadd or Rasm
Classification of definitions into complete/incomplete hadd/rasm.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given definition as a complete hadd, incomplete hadd, complete rasm, or incomplete rasm based on its composition.

Core Idea
A definition's type depends on whether its parts are essential or accidental, and whether the genus and differentia are close or remote.

In this lesson, we explore the classification system for definitions in Islamic logic. The author presents a clear hierarchy: definitions are either hadd (essential) or rasm (accidental), and each can be complete or incomplete. Understanding this classification is crucial for constructing valid definitions and for critiquing definitions in theological and legal debates.

Guided Path
1
Identify the genus and distinguishing part
Find the two main parts of the definition: the broader category (genus) and the distinguishing feature (differentia or property).
The genus is the common category; the distinguishing part is what sets it apart.
2
Determine if the parts are essential (dhatiyyat) or accidental (ardiyyat)
Essential parts enter the quiddity; accidental parts do not. Essential parts make it a hadd; accidental parts make it a rasm.
3
Check the closeness of the genus
A close genus (jins qarib) is the immediate category above the definiendum; a remote genus is further up.
E.g., for 'human', 'animal' is close; 'body' is remote.
4
For hadd: check if both parts are essential and the differentia is close
A complete hadd requires close genus and close differentia. An incomplete hadd has a close differentia but a remote genus, or the differentia alone.
If only the differentia is given without genus, it is incomplete.
5
For rasm: check if the property (khassa) and genus are close
A complete rasm is composed of a close genus and a property. An incomplete rasm lacks one or both.
A property is an accidental distinguishing feature, e.g., 'laughing' for humans.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقيل كون ناقض التعريف مستدلا حاصل إذا لم يدع صاحب التعريف حقيقيا أو إسميا لا لفظيا إذ الحدية والرسمية لا يجرى فيه بأن هذا التعريف حد تاما أو ناقصا أو رسم كذلك. فإذا ادعى صاحب التعريف أنه حد فكأنه ادعى أن العام والخاص اللذين ذكرا فيه من الذاتيات فيسمى العام جنسا قريبا أو بعيدا والخاص فصلا قريبا أو بعيدا مطلقا سواء كان حدا تاما أو ناقصا. وإذا ادعى صاحب التعريف أنه رسم فكأنه ادعى أن أحدهما أو كليهما من العرضيات فيسمى العام جنسا قريبا إن تاما وبعيدا أو فصلا بعيدا على مذهب أو عرضا عاما على رأي إن ناقصا والجزء الخاص خاصة إن تاما وخاصة أو فصلا قريبا أو عرضا عاما على زعم إن ناقصا. فيجوز الاعتراض بمنع كونهما من الذاتيات مطلقا وبمنع كونهما مجموع الذاتيات أو بعضها إن قيده بالتام أو بالناقص. وأما عند الادعاء بالرسم فيعترض عليه بمنع كون أحدهما أو كليهما من العرضيات أى بمنع عدم كون كل الجزءين من الذاتيات وكذا يعترض عليه بمنع تركب ذلك التعريف من الجنس القريب والخاصة إن قيده بالتام وبمنع عدم تركب منهما إن قيده بالناقص. هذا هو المشهور. وقال سيد المحققين وعماد المدققين إن التعريف إن كان مركبا من الجنس والفصل القريبين فحد تام وإلا فان كان بالفصل القريب وحده أو مركبه مع الغير فحد ناقص وإلا فان كان مركبا من الجنس القريب والخاصة فرسم تام وإلا فرسم ناقص.
And it is said that the critic of the definition being a 'mustadill' holds when the owner of the definition—whether real or nominal, not verbal, since 'had diyya' and 'rasmiyya' do not apply to it—does not claim that this definition is a complete or incomplete hadd or a complete or incomplete rasm. So if the owner of the definition claims that it is a hadd, then it is as if he claimed that the universal (al-amm) and the particular (al-khass) mentioned in it are from the essential properties (al-dhatiyyat); thus the universal is called a genus (jins) close or remote, and the particular is called a differentia (fasl) close or remote, absolutely, whether the definition is a complete or incomplete hadd. And if the owner of the definition claims that it is a rasm, then it is as if he claimed that one or both of them are from the accidental properties (al-ardiyyat); thus the universal is called a genus close if complete, or remote or a remote differentia according to some, or a general accident according to another view if incomplete; and the particular is called a property (khassa) if complete, and a property or a close differentia or a general accident according to some if incomplete. So it is permissible to object by preventing that both are from the essential properties absolutely, and by preventing that they are the sum of essential properties or some of them if he restricts it by complete or incomplete. And as for when claiming it is a rasm, one objects to him by preventing that one or both are from the accidental properties, i.e., by preventing that both parts are not from the essential properties. Similarly, one objects to him by preventing that definition is composed of the close genus and the property if he restricts it by complete, and by preventing it is not composed of them if he restricts it by incomplete. This is the well-known view. And the sayyid of the investigators and the pillar of the verifiers said: The definition, if it is composed of the close genus and the close differentia, then it is a complete hadd; otherwise, if it is by the close differentia alone or with something else, then it is an incomplete hadd; otherwise, if it is composed of the close genus and the property, then it is a complete rasm; otherwise it is an incomplete rasm.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage explains how the roles of critic and defender shift based on the definer's claim about the type of definition. It begins by noting that the earlier definition of a critic as a mustadill only holds when the definer does not explicitly claim that his definition is a hadd (definition by essential properties) or rasm (definition by accidental properties). If the definer claims it is a hadd, then implicitly he is claiming that the two parts (the general and the specific) mentioned are both essential (dhatiyyat). The general part is then a genus, and the specific part is a differentia. The critic can then object by denying that one or both parts are essential. Similarly, if the definer claims it is a rasm, he is claiming that at least one part is accidental (ardiyyat). The critic can then object by denying that they are accidental. The passage then gives a hierarchy: a definition composed of close genus and close differentia is a complete hadd; if only the close differentia (alone or with something else) it is an incomplete hadd; if composed of close genus and property it is a complete rasm; otherwise an incomplete rasm. This classification is crucial for understanding how definitions are judged in the Islamic logical tradition. For example, defining 'human' as 'rational animal' is a complete hadd (genus: animal, differentia: rational). Defining it as 'featherless biped' would be a rasm (an accidental description). The critic must know which claim the definer is making to frame his objection appropriately.

Key Terms
حد
رسم
ذاتيات
عرضيات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what composition makes a definition a complete hadd?
A) Close genus and close differentia
B) Close genus and property
C) Remote differentia alone
D) Any two parts one essential one accidental

Key Takeaway
A definition's type is determined by whether its parts are essential or accidental and whether the genus and distinguishing feature are close or remote.

Formulating Objections Based on the Definer's Claim
Objection strategies based on definer's claim.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to formulate appropriate objections to a definition given the definer's claim that it is a hadd or rasm.

Core Idea
The critic's objection must match the definer's implicit claim about the nature of the parts.

When a definer explicitly states that his definition is a hadd or rasm, he implicitly commits to a certain composition of parts. The critic can then object by denying that the parts meet those commitments. This lesson explores the specific objections allowed for each case.

Guided Path
1
Determine the definer's explicit claim (hadd or rasm)
The critic must first note what the definer claims his definition is.
The claim can be 'complete hadd', 'incomplete hadd', 'complete rasm', or 'incomplete rasm'.
2
If claim is hadd, object that the parts are not essential
The critic can deny that both parts are from dhatiyyat, or that they are the sum of essential parts (if complete) or some (if incomplete).
3
If claim is rasm, object that the parts are not accidental
The critic can deny that one or both parts are from ardiyyat, meaning they are not both non-essential.
4
For complete rasm, object that it is composed of neither close genus nor property
If the definer claims complete rasm, the critic can deny it is composed of close genus and property.
5
For incomplete rasm, object that it is not lacking that composition
If the definer claims incomplete rasm, the critic can deny that it lacks that composition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And it is said that the critic of the definition being a 'mustadill' holds when the owner of the definition—whether real or nominal, not verbal, since 'had diyya' and 'rasmiyya' do not apply to it—does not claim that this definition is a complete or incomplete hadd or a complete or incomplete rasm. Commentary
This passage explains how the roles of critic and defender shift based on the definer's claim about the type of definition. It begins by noting that the earlier definition of a critic as a mustadill only holds when the definer does not explicitly claim that his definition is a hadd (definition by essential properties) or rasm (definition by accidental properties). If the definer claims it is a hadd, then implicitly he is claiming that the two parts (the general and the specific) mentioned are both essential (dhatiyyat). The general part is then a genus, and the specific part is a differentia. The critic can then object by denying that one or both parts are essential. Similarly, if the definer claims it is a rasm, he is claiming that at least one part is accidental (ardiyyat). The critic can then object by denying that they are accidental. The passage then gives a hierarchy: a definition composed of close genus and close differentia is a complete hadd; if only the close differentia (alone or with something else) it is an incomplete hadd; if composed of close genus and property it is a complete rasm; otherwise an incomplete rasm. This classification is crucial for understanding how definitions are judged in the Islamic logical tradition. For example, defining 'human' as 'rational animal' is a complete hadd (genus: animal, differentia: rational). Defining it as 'featherless biped' would be a rasm (an accidental description). The critic must know which claim the definer is making to frame his objection appropriately.

ناقض
مستدل
منع
ذاتيات
عرضيات

If a definer claims his definition is a complete rasm, what specific objection is allowed according to the passage?
A) Object that the parts are not essential
B) Object that the definition is not composed of close genus and property

Key Takeaway
The critic's objections are tailored to the definer's claim: for hadd, challenge essentialness; for rasm, challenge accidentalness; and for complete/incomplete forms, challenge the presence or absence of specific components.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Hadd and Rasm
advanced
10 min
Implicit Claims in Definitions: Da'wa Dimniyya
Author explains how claims of hadd or rasm imply specific component types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit claim (da'wa dimniyya) underlying a given definition and determine whether a valid objection (man') targets the wording or the claim.

Core Idea
The real target of objection is the implicit claim, not the definition itself.

When a scholar presents a definition, he often claims it is a complete or incomplete hadd or rasm. But the definition itself is not directly debatable—what is debatable is the implicit claim that its components are essential or accidental. This lesson unpacks the logic of that implicit claim and shows you how to properly object in a dialectical setting.

Guided Path
1
Recall the types of definitions
Review hadd (complete/incomplete) and rasm (complete/incomplete). Hadd uses essential components (genus and differentia); rasm uses accidental components (genus and property or general accident).
Complete hadd = proximate genus + proximate differentia. Incomplete hadd = remote genus or differentia alone.
2
Extract the implicit claim from a definition
Given a claim like 'This definition is a complete hadd', the implicit claim is that both parts (the general and the specific) are essential (dhati). For a rasm claim, at least one part is accidental ('aradi).
The implicit claim is not stated but logically follows from the type of definition claimed.
3
Identify the correct locus of objection
The objection (man') must target the implicit claim, not the wording of the definition. Objecting to the wording itself is like preventing an engraver while he engraves—absurd.
The passage emphasizes that the definition itself has no judgment, so it cannot be prevented; only the implied assertion can.
4
Apply to a real Islamic definition
Take a definition from usul al-fiqh: 'Salah is specific sayings and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' claimed as a complete hadd. The implicit claim is that 'specific sayings and actions' (genus) and 'beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' (differentia) are essential. A valid objection would be: 'I deny that the genus is essential because praying in certain conditions may not require all sayings.'
The objection does not attack the wording but the essential status of the components.

And it was said: The status of the one refuting the definition as providing evidence holds if the owner of the definition does not claim—from the claim—a real or nominal [definition], not a verbal one, because [the status of] being a complete definition (hadd) or a description (rasm) does not proceed in it, [such as] that this definition is a complete or incomplete hadd or a rasm likewise. So if the owner of the definition claims that it is a hadd absolutely, then it is as if the owner of the definition claimed that the general mentioned first and the specific mentioned second, which are mentioned in it—i.e., in the definition—are from the essential properties (dhatiyyat). And the essential is that which enters into the reality of its particulars, so the species is excluded from it—this is apparent because the species does not occur in the definition except with far-fetched effort—or that which is not external to the reality of its particulars, so the species enters into it. So the general is called a proximate or remote genus, and the specific is called a proximate, rather even remote, differentia absolutely, whether it is a complete or incomplete hadd. And if the owner of the definition claims that it—i.e., the definition—is a rasm, then it is as if the owner of the definition claimed that one of them—i.e., one of the general and specific—if the rasm is complete because it is composed of the proximate genus and necessary property, or both of them if the rasm is incomplete, are from the accidental properties ('aradiyyat). And the accidental is that which exits from the reality of its particulars. When one of these claims issues from the owner of the definition, objection (i'tirad) is permissible from the side of the questioner, just as objection is permissible by what preceded: by preventing them from being—i.e., by preventing each of the general and specific, or preventing one of them, since prevention of it is sufficient—from the essential properties. That is on the assumption that the definition is a complete or incomplete hadd or a complete or incomplete rasm, so understand. And since the locus of the prevention understood from what preceded is more general than the explicit locus, he said in the gloss: i.e., the status of the one refuting the definition as providing evidence concluded. And likewise the status of its corrector as merely preventing, when the owner of the definition does not claim that this definition is a hadd or rasm. But at the time of claim, it may be the opposite. Therefore he said: So if he claims that it is a hadd, then it is as if he claimed that each one of the general and specific mentioned in it is from the essential—'min' is partitive. As for the claim that they are the totality of the essential or some of it, it does not follow unless he restricted it with [the terms] complete or incomplete. So the general part is called a proximate genus if complete, and a remote genus or remote differentia according to a school if incomplete, and the specific part is called a proximate differentia absolutely. And if the owner of the definition claims that it is a rasm, then it is as if he claimed that one of them or both are from the accidental—meaning that this definition is not merely the essential. So the general part is called a proximate genus if complete, and remote or remote differentia according to a school, or a general accident according to an opinion if incomplete; and the specific part is called a property if complete, and a property or proximate differentia or general accident according to a claim if incomplete. So objection is permissible by preventing both of them—i.e., each of them—from being essential absolutely, and by preventing them from being the totality of the essential or some of it if he restricted it with complete or incomplete. This is at the claim of hadd. As for at the claim of rasm, objection is made against him by preventing one of them or both from being accidental—i.e., by preventing that each of the two parts is not from the essential. Likewise objection is made against him by preventing the composition of that definition from the proximate genus and property if he restricted it with complete, and by preventing its non-composition from them if he restricted it with incomplete. This is the well-known. And the chief of the verifiers and pillar of the scrutinizers said: The definition, if it is composed of the proximate genus and differentia, it is a complete hadd; otherwise, if it is by the proximate differentia alone or combined with something else, it is an incomplete hadd; otherwise, if it is composed of the proximate genus and property, it is a complete rasm; otherwise it is an incomplete rasm. And the implicit [claim] in the first look, and the intended meaning was the explicit locus, he clarified the intended meaning to remove that generality and said: And the locus of this prevention here—i.e., in the situation of the owner of the definition claiming one of these claims—is the implicit claim, on the basis that it is permissible for it to be of the type of connecting the cause to the effect, reflect. And the locus is only the implicit claim because the prevention does not apply to the wording of the definition; otherwise preventing it would be like preventing the engraving of the engraver while he is engraving. Yes, if one considers in the definition that this definition is correspondent to the defined, then prevention applies to it, as is not hidden. And the summary is that the definition itself is not susceptible to prevention except by consideration of the explicit or implicit claim, because prevention requires a judgment and there is no judgment in definitions. For if you say: Just as prevention applies by consideration of the implicit claim, so too it applies by consideration of the explicit claim, so why did the author suffice with it? I say: If the state of the implicit claim is known, the state of the explicit claim is known by the primary method. Yes, refutation (naqd) applies to the exclusivity derived from silence and [...], unless it is said that the exclusivity is relative, not real. So know, an indication that the prevention applied to the implicit claim is a figurative prevention linguistically, and it would only be a real prevention if the prevention applied to a specific premise. And it is possible that it is an indication that just as prevention and refutation apply to the definition, so too does opposition (mu'arada) apply to it, such as that this definition, for example, is opposed to that definition, so it is invalid; and to the answer that opposition to the definition is known by comparison or left entirely due to its rarity. And that it is an indication that if the prevention applied to one of these claims, it would not apply to the definition, so the exception is not correct by his saying: But this. And repelling this mentioned prevention is only by proving that each of them or one of them is from the essential, or proving that each of them or one of them is from the accidental. And this—i.e., proving essentialness and accidentalness—is difficult or impossible, because it was said that distinguishing the essential—whether genus or differentia—from the accidental—whether property or general accident—is difficult. And it is a proof from the first figure, such that one says: This proof is difficult because it depends on distinguishing the essential from the accidental, and distinguishing the essential from the accidental is difficult; so this proof depends on what is difficult, and what depends on what is difficult is difficult. And the reason for its being difficult is that the genus resembles the general accident, and the differentia resembles the property, so distinguishing them is difficult—beneath it is cutting the tough plant. But this is only in existent realities and real definitions. As for conceptual definitions, distinguishing them is easy because it is based on the conventions of the practitioners of the arts. So what they consider as included, it is essential; otherwise it is accidental. For example, if the grammarians define and consider [a term as included]... And the locus of prevention here is truly the implicit claim derived from the definer's saying 'define it' or 'describe it' for example. So it is replied that objection against it is not an objection against the definition, so the exception is not correct unless it is said that the definition's locus is customary. And when the customary is considered, the exception is needed. Perhaps his saying 'so know' is an indication of this, but there is [something] in it. And repelling this prevention is only by proving essentialness for the general and specific, so at the prevention of being a hadd, or by proving accidentalness for them or for one of them at the prevention of being a rasm. And this proof is very difficult in existent realities because it was said that distinguishing the essential from the accidental is difficult to the point of impossibility, because the remote genus and differentia and the general accident are similar. Likewise the proximate differentia and the property, so the distinction between them is extremely difficult. But since the matter is conjectural (dhanni), demanding proof of it is not an impossible task. As for linguistic and conventional concepts, it is easy for one who is qualified, because the word, when placed in the language or in the convention...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion about the nature of definitions (hadd and rasm) and the proper way to object to them in debate. It begins by clarifying that when a definer puts forward a definition, he implicitly makes claims about its components. If he claims it is a hadd (complete or incomplete), he implicitly claims that the general part (al-'amm) is a genus (jins) and the specific part (al-khass) is a differentia (fasl), both of which are essential (dhati). If he claims it is a rasm (complete or incomplete), he implicitly claims that one or both parts are accidental ('aradi), i.e., a property (khassa) or general accident ('arad 'amm). The text then explains the valid objection (man') that the questioner can raise: he can prevent (man') the parts from being essential or accidental, depending on the claim. The passage then delves into a crucial refinement: the prevention is not directly against the wording of the definition but against the implicit claim (da'wa dimniyya) underlying it. This is because the definition itself is a statement, not a judgment, so it cannot be prevented directly. The author supports this by analogy: objecting to the definition itself would be like preventing an engraver from engraving while he is engraving—absurd. Instead, the prevention targets the implied assertion that the parts are essential or accidental. The passage then discusses how to repel the objection: by proving that the parts are indeed essential or accidental. This proof is difficult, especially for real definitions of existent things, because distinguishing essential from accidental features is hard—genus and differentia resemble general accident and property. The author gives a formal categorical syllogism in the first figure to demonstrate the difficulty: proof depends on distinguishing essential from accidental, and that distinction is difficult, so the proof is difficult. However, for conventional or terminological definitions (e.g., in grammar or jurisprudence), the task is easier because it relies on the conventions of the specialists. The passage ends by emphasizing that the actual locus of prevention is the implicit claim, and that repelling it requires proof, which is arduous in real essences but manageable in conventional terms.

Checkpoint
A scholar defines 'salah' as 'specific statements and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim' and claims it is a complete hadd. Based on the passage, what implicit claim is he making about 'specific statements and actions' and 'beginning with takbir and ending with taslim'?
A) Both parts are accidental properties.
B) Both parts are essential properties (dhati).
C) One part is essential and the other accidental.
D) The definition is nominal only.

Key Takeaway
The real target of objection in definitional debates is the implicit claim (da'wa dimniyya) about the components being essential or accidental, not the wording itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

Distinguishing Essential from Accidental: A Categorical Proof of Difficulty
Author presents a categorical syllogism proving the difficulty of distinguishing essential from accidental.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the categorical syllogism showing the difficulty of proving essentialness/accidentalness and evaluate its validity.

Core Idea
The distinction between essential and accidental is difficult, making proof of definitions conjectural for real essences.

Proving that a component of a definition is essential or accidental is not trivial. The author presents a compact categorical syllogism in the first figure to demonstrate that such proof is inherently difficult when dealing with real definitions. He also contrasts this with conventional definitions, where the task is easier. This lesson walks you through that syllogism and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion of the syllogism
The conclusion the author aims to prove is: 'This proof [of essentialness/accidentalness] is difficult' (عسير). This is the claim that defending a definitional claim is hard.
The proof referred to is the act of proving that the parts are indeed essential or accidental.
2
Find the premises
The author states: 'because it depends on distinguishing the essential from the accidental' and 'distinguishing the essential from the accidental is difficult' (عسير). These are the two premises of the syllogism.
The first premise: 'Proof of essentialness/accidentalness depends on distinguishing essential from accidental.' The second: 'Distinguishing essential from accidental is difficult.'
3
Construct the categorical first-figure syllogism
Putting the premises together: Major premise: 'Everything that depends on something difficult is difficult.' Minor premise: 'This proof depends on something difficult (i.e., distinguishing).' Therefore, 'This proof is difficult.' The author explicitly says this is a proof (دليل) from the first figure (الشكل الأول).
In first-figure form: The middle term is 'depends on something difficult'? Actually, the author writes: 'This proof is difficult because it depends on distinguishing, and distinguishing is difficult.' This can be formalized as: (1) Whatever depends on a difficult thing is difficult. (2) This proof depends on a difficult thing (distinguishing). (3) Therefore, this proof is difficult.
4
Distinguish real vs conventional definitions
The author notes that this difficulty applies only to real definitions (الحقائق الموجودة والتعاريف الحقيقية). For conventional definitions (المفهومات الاعتبارية), such as those in grammar or jurisprudence, the distinction is easy because it relies on the conventions of the specialists.
Example: In grammar, what is considered essential (dhati) is defined by the practitioners, so there is no metaphysical difficulty.
