And it was said: The status of the one refuting the definition as providing evidence holds if the owner of the definition does not claim—from the claim—a real or nominal [definition], not a verbal one, because [the status of] being a complete definition (hadd) or a description (rasm) does not proceed in it, [such as] that this definition is a complete or incomplete hadd or a rasm likewise. So if the owner of the definition claims that it is a hadd absolutely, then it is as if the owner of the definition claimed that the general mentioned first and the specific mentioned second, which are mentioned in it—i.e., in the definition—are from the essential properties (dhatiyyat). And the essential is that which enters into the reality of its particulars, so the species is excluded from it—this is apparent because the species does not occur in the definition except with far-fetched effort—or that which is not external to the reality of its particulars, so the species enters into it. So the general is called a proximate or remote genus, and the specific is called a proximate, rather even remote, differentia absolutely, whether it is a complete or incomplete hadd. And if the owner of the definition claims that it—i.e., the definition—is a rasm, then it is as if the owner of the definition claimed that one of them—i.e., one of the general and specific—if the rasm is complete because it is composed of the proximate genus and necessary property, or both of them if the rasm is incomplete, are from the accidental properties ('aradiyyat). And the accidental is that which exits from the reality of its particulars. When one of these claims issues from the owner of the definition, objection (i'tirad) is permissible from the side of the questioner, just as objection is permissible by what preceded: by preventing them from being—i.e., by preventing each of the general and specific, or preventing one of them, since prevention of it is sufficient—from the essential properties. That is on the assumption that the definition is a complete or incomplete hadd or a complete or incomplete rasm, so understand. And since the locus of the prevention understood from what preceded is more general than the explicit locus, he said in the gloss: i.e., the status of the one refuting the definition as providing evidence concluded. And likewise the status of its corrector as merely preventing, when the owner of the definition does not claim that this definition is a hadd or rasm. But at the time of claim, it may be the opposite. Therefore he said: So if he claims that it is a hadd, then it is as if he claimed that each one of the general and specific mentioned in it is from the essential—'min' is partitive. As for the claim that they are the totality of the essential or some of it, it does not follow unless he restricted it with [the terms] complete or incomplete. So the general part is called a proximate genus if complete, and a remote genus or remote differentia according to a school if incomplete, and the specific part is called a proximate differentia absolutely. And if the owner of the definition claims that it is a rasm, then it is as if he claimed that one of them or both are from the accidental—meaning that this definition is not merely the essential. So the general part is called a proximate genus if complete, and remote or remote differentia according to a school, or a general accident according to an opinion if incomplete; and the specific part is called a property if complete, and a property or proximate differentia or general accident according to a claim if incomplete. So objection is permissible by preventing both of them—i.e., each of them—from being essential absolutely, and by preventing them from being the totality of the essential or some of it if he restricted it with complete or incomplete. This is at the claim of hadd. As for at the claim of rasm, objection is made against him by preventing one of them or both from being accidental—i.e., by preventing that each of the two parts is not from the essential. Likewise objection is made against him by preventing the composition of that definition from the proximate genus and property if he restricted it with complete, and by preventing its non-composition from them if he restricted it with incomplete. This is the well-known. And the chief of the verifiers and pillar of the scrutinizers said: The definition, if it is composed of the proximate genus and differentia, it is a complete hadd; otherwise, if it is by the proximate differentia alone or combined with something else, it is an incomplete hadd; otherwise, if it is composed of the proximate genus and property, it is a complete rasm; otherwise it is an incomplete rasm. And the implicit [claim] in the first look, and the intended meaning was the explicit locus, he clarified the intended meaning to remove that generality and said: And the locus of this prevention here—i.e., in the situation of the owner of the definition claiming one of these claims—is the implicit claim, on the basis that it is permissible for it to be of the type of connecting the cause to the effect, reflect. And the locus is only the implicit claim because the prevention does not apply to the wording of the definition; otherwise preventing it would be like preventing the engraving of the engraver while he is engraving. Yes, if one considers in the definition that this definition is correspondent to the defined, then prevention applies to it, as is not hidden. And the summary is that the definition itself is not susceptible to prevention except by consideration of the explicit or implicit claim, because prevention requires a judgment and there is no judgment in definitions. For if you say: Just as prevention applies by consideration of the implicit claim, so too it applies by consideration of the explicit claim, so why did the author suffice with it? I say: If the state of the implicit claim is known, the state of the explicit claim is known by the primary method. Yes, refutation (naqd) applies to the exclusivity derived from silence and [...], unless it is said that the exclusivity is relative, not real. So know, an indication that the prevention applied to the implicit claim is a figurative prevention linguistically, and it would only be a real prevention if the prevention applied to a specific premise. And it is possible that it is an indication that just as prevention and refutation apply to the definition, so too does opposition (mu'arada) apply to it, such as that this definition, for example, is opposed to that definition, so it is invalid; and to the answer that opposition to the definition is known by comparison or left entirely due to its rarity. And that it is an indication that if the prevention applied to one of these claims, it would not apply to the definition, so the exception is not correct by his saying: But this. And repelling this mentioned prevention is only by proving that each of them or one of them is from the essential, or proving that each of them or one of them is from the accidental. And this—i.e., proving essentialness and accidentalness—is difficult or impossible, because it was said that distinguishing the essential—whether genus or differentia—from the accidental—whether property or general accident—is difficult. And it is a proof from the first figure, such that one says: This proof is difficult because it depends on distinguishing the essential from the accidental, and distinguishing the essential from the accidental is difficult; so this proof depends on what is difficult, and what depends on what is difficult is difficult. And the reason for its being difficult is that the genus resembles the general accident, and the differentia resembles the property, so distinguishing them is difficult—beneath it is cutting the tough plant. But this is only in existent realities and real definitions. As for conceptual definitions, distinguishing them is easy because it is based on the conventions of the practitioners of the arts. So what they consider as included, it is essential; otherwise it is accidental. For example, if the grammarians define and consider [a term as included]... And the locus of prevention here is truly the implicit claim derived from the definer's saying 'define it' or 'describe it' for example. So it is replied that objection against it is not an objection against the definition, so the exception is not correct unless it is said that the definition's locus is customary. And when the customary is considered, the exception is needed. Perhaps his saying 'so know' is an indication of this, but there is [something] in it. And repelling this prevention is only by proving essentialness for the general and specific, so at the prevention of being a hadd, or by proving accidentalness for them or for one of them at the prevention of being a rasm. And this proof is very difficult in existent realities because it was said that distinguishing the essential from the accidental is difficult to the point of impossibility, because the remote genus and differentia and the general accident are similar. Likewise the proximate differentia and the property, so the distinction between them is extremely difficult. But since the matter is conjectural (dhanni), demanding proof of it is not an impossible task. As for linguistic and conventional concepts, it is easy for one who is qualified, because the word, when placed in the language or in the convention...
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage is an extended discussion about the nature of definitions (hadd and rasm) and the proper way to object to them in debate. It begins by clarifying that when a definer puts forward a definition, he implicitly makes claims about its components. If he claims it is a hadd (complete or incomplete), he implicitly claims that the general part (al-'amm) is a genus (jins) and the specific part (al-khass) is a differentia (fasl), both of which are essential (dhati). If he claims it is a rasm (complete or incomplete), he implicitly claims that one or both parts are accidental ('aradi), i.e., a property (khassa) or general accident ('arad 'amm). The text then explains the valid objection (man') that the questioner can raise: he can prevent (man') the parts from being essential or accidental, depending on the claim. The passage then delves into a crucial refinement: the prevention is not directly against the wording of the definition but against the implicit claim (da'wa dimniyya) underlying it. This is because the definition itself is a statement, not a judgment, so it cannot be prevented directly. The author supports this by analogy: objecting to the definition itself would be like preventing an engraver from engraving while he is engraving—absurd. Instead, the prevention targets the implied assertion that the parts are essential or accidental. The passage then discusses how to repel the objection: by proving that the parts are indeed essential or accidental. This proof is difficult, especially for real definitions of existent things, because distinguishing essential from accidental features is hard—genus and differentia resemble general accident and property. The author gives a formal categorical syllogism in the first figure to demonstrate the difficulty: proof depends on distinguishing essential from accidental, and that distinction is difficult, so the proof is difficult. However, for conventional or terminological definitions (e.g., in grammar or jurisprudence), the task is easier because it relies on the conventions of the specialists. The passage ends by emphasizing that the actual locus of prevention is the implicit claim, and that repelling it requires proof, which is arduous in real essences but manageable in conventional terms.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what is the main reason that proving essentialness or accidentalness is difficult in real definitions?
A) The definitions are too long.
B) The terms are ambiguous in the Arabic language.
C) Genus resembles general accident and differentia resembles property.
D) The evidence for the claim is always lacking.

Key Takeaway
Proof of definitional claims is difficult for real essences because essential and accidental features resemble each other, but it is easy for conventional definitions where expert consensus determines what is essential.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Construct the categorical syllogism from the passage in the first figure. Use the following premises: 'Distinguishing essential from accidental is difficult' and 'Proof of definitional claims depends on this distinction.' Conclude that the proof is difficult.
?
Take a definition from fiqh: 'Riba is an increase in a specific type of commodity' claimed as a complete hadd. Using the syllogism, explain why proving that 'increase' is the essence (genus) and 'in a specific type' is the differentia is difficult in real terms but easier if we treat it as a conventional definition.

Hadd and Rasm
intermediate
5 min
Objection Targets the Implicit Claim in Definitions
Author explains that objection to a definition actually targets the implicit claim, not the definition itself.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the implicit claim underlying any definition and explain why objections must target that claim rather than the definition itself.

Core Idea
An objection to a definition is really an objection to its hidden claim of essentiality.

When a scholar presents a definition, they are not just stating words—they are implicitly claiming that those words capture the essence of the thing defined. This passage from the commentary explains why a skilled disputant (munazir) must direct objections to that implicit claim, not to the definition as a whole. Learning this principle sharpens your ability to evaluate definitions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Separate definition from implicit claim
Take any definition, e.g., 'Man is a rational animal.' The definition itself is a statement. The implicit claim is that 'rational animal' consists of the essential genus (animal) and differentia (rational), and that it matches the reality of man.
The implicit claim is not stated but is assumed by the definer.
2
Recognize why definitions resist direct objection
A definition simply describes; it does not assert a proposition that can be true or false. Thus, saying 'this definition is wrong' is like saying 'the drawing is wrong' while ignoring that the draftsman drew it. The objection must have a target that can be judged.
The passage says: 'the definition itself is not subject to objection except by consideration of the explicit or implicit claim, because objection requires a judgment.'
3
Apply the principle to an Islamic science example
Consider a usuli definition: 'Sijill (record) is a document that preserves testimony.' The implicit claim is that 'preserving testimony' is the essential function. An objector could say: 'But a record does not always preserve testimony; sometimes it preserves contracts.' This targets the implicit claim about essentiality.
The objector does not reject the definition outright, but challenges the underlying claim.
4
Identify the figurative nature of the objection
The commentator notes that the objection on the implicit claim is 'figurative' because the real objection would be on a specific premise like 'this is the genus.' The phrase 'so know' signals this nuance.
Understanding this prevents confusion in debates.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
والضمني في أول النظرة، وكان المراد المورد [؟] صرح بالمراد إزالة لذلك العموم فقال (ومورد هذا المنع هنا) أي في مقام ادعاء صاحب التعريف أحد هذه الدعاوي (الدعوى الضمنية) على أنه يجوز أن يكون من قبيل عطف العلة على المعلول تأمل. وإنما كان المورد الدعوى الضمنية لأن المنع لا يرد على نفس التعريف وإلا لكان منعه بمنزلة منع نقش النقاش وهو ينقش. نعم لو اعتبر في التعريف أن هذا التعريف مطابق للمعرف فيرد عليه المنع كما لا يخفى. والحاصل أن نفس التعريف غير قابل للمنع إلا باعتبار الدعوى الصريحة أو الضمنية لأن المنع يقتضي الحكم ولا حكم في التعريفات. فان قلت كما أن المنع يرد باعتبار الدعوى الضمنية فكذلك يرد باعتبار الدعوى الصريحة فلم اكتفى المصنف بها. قلت إذا علم حال الدعوى الضمنية علم حال الصريحة بالطريق الأولى. نعم يرد النقض على الحصر المستفاد من السكوت والقاسم، اللهم إلا أن يقال الحصر إضافي لا حقيقي. (فاعرف) إشارة إلى أن المنع الوارد على الدعوى الضمنية منع مجاز لغوي، وإنما يكون منعا حقيقيا لو كان المنع واردا على مقدمة معينة.
And the implicit [claim] at first glance, and the intended meaning was the [?] clarified the intended meaning to remove that generality, so he said: 'And the place of this objection here' — i.e., in the context of the definer claiming one of these claims — 'is the implicit claim', although it is possible that it is of the type of coordinating the cause with the effect, reflect. And the place of objection is only the implicit claim because the objection does not apply to the definition itself; otherwise its rejection would be like rejecting the drawing of the draftsman while he is drawing. Yes, if it were considered in the definition that this definition corresponds to the definiendum, then objection would apply to it, as is not hidden. The upshot is that the definition itself is not subject to objection except by consideration of the explicit or implicit claim, because objection requires a judgment, and there is no judgment in definitions. If you say: Just as objection applies by consideration of the implicit claim, so too it applies by consideration of the explicit claim, so why did the author suffice with it? I say: If the state of the implicit claim is known, the state of the explicit is known a fortiori. Yes, a counterexample applies to the exclusivity derived from silence and ? unless it is said that the exclusivity is relative, not real. (So know) is an indication that the objection applied to the implicit claim is a figurative objection; it is only a real objection if it is applied to a specific premise.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage discusses the nature of objection (man') in definitional contexts. The author explains that an objection cannot be directly levied against a definition itself, because a definition merely states the essence or description of something and does not assert a judgment that can be true or false. Instead, the objection targets the implicit claim (da'wa dimniyya) that the definer makes when presenting the definition. For example, when someone says 'Define man as rational animal,' the implicit claim is that 'rational animal' is a proper definition consisting of essential parts (genus and differentia). The objector can challenge this implicit claim by denying that the genus is truly essential or that the differentia is correct. The author clarifies that the author of the text suffices with discussing the implicit claim because the explicit claim follows a fortiori. He also notes that an objection is only real if it is directed at a specific premise, such as 'this is the genus,' rather than at the definition as a whole. The phrase 'so know' signals that the objection on the implicit claim is figurative in the sense that it is not a direct refutation of the definition but of its underlying presupposition.

Key Terms
منع
الدعوى الضمنية

Key Terms from Source Passage

According to this passage, why can an objection not be directed at a definition itself?
A) Because definitions are always true.
B) Because definitions contain no judgment that can be true or false.
C) Because definitions are too vague.
D) Because objections are only allowed in syllogisms.

Key Takeaway
An objection to a definition is always an objection to the implicit claim that the definition captures the essence, not to the definition's wording.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Then formulate an objection that targets that implicit claim rather than the definition itself. Use an example from fiqh (e.g., a drink made from dates that also intoxicates).

The Difficulty of Proving Essentiality in Real Definitions
Author presents a syllogism on the difficulty of proving essentiality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to reconstruct the first-figure syllogism proving the difficulty of establishing essentiality and identify the root cause in the resemblance between genus and common accident.

Core Idea
Proving essentiality is hard because genus and differentia look like common accident and property.

In this passage, the author presents a first-figure syllogism to show that proving whether a part of a definition is essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi) is extremely difficult for real essences. The core reason is that genus resembles common accident, and differentia resembles property, making them hard to distinguish. This lesson unpacks that argument and highlights the exception for conventional concepts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conclusion being argued
The conclusion is that proving essentiality or accidentally is 'difficult or impossible' (عسير أو متعذر). This is what the syllogism aims to establish.
2
Find the premises of the first-figure syllogism
The author presents: 'This proof depends on distinguishing essential from accidental; distinguishing essential from accidental is difficult; therefore this proof is difficult.' Recognize that this is a categorical syllogism in Barbara mood.
3
Understand why distinguishing is hard
The reason given is that genus (jins) resembles common accident (arad 'amm), and differentia (fasl) resembles property (khassa). The similarity makes differentiation extremely difficult, 'harder than cutting the qatad' (an Arabian thorn).
4
Note the exception: conventional concepts
The author says that for conceptual or linguistic matters (المفهومات الاعتبارية), distinguishing is easy because the boundaries are set by the conventions of the practitioners of that art. Whatever they include is considered essential.

And repelling this mentioned objection is only by proving that each of the two or one of them is from the essential, or proving that each of the two or one of them is from the accidental. And this — i.e., proving essentiality and accidentally — is difficult or impossible, because it has been said that distinguishing the essential (whether genus or differentia) from the accidental (whether property or common accident) is difficult. And it is a proof in the first figure: This proof is difficult because it depends on distinguishing essential from accidental, and distinguishing essential from accidental is difficult; thus this proof depends on what is difficult, and what depends on what is difficult is difficult. The reason for its difficulty is that the genus resembles the common accident, and the differentia resembles the property, so distinguishing them is difficult, harder than cutting the [؟]... But this is only in existing realities and real definitions. As for conceptual matters, distinguishing them is easy because it is based on the conventions of the masters of the arts: what they consider as internal is essential, otherwise it is accidental. For example, if the grammarians define ... and the place of objection here is truly the implicit claim derived from the definer's saying 'they defined it' or 'they described it' etc. So the objection against it is not an objection against the definition, so the exception is not valid unless it is said that the definition's place is customary. And since the customary is what is considered, the exception was needed. Perhaps his saying 'so know' is an indication of this, but it has what it has. And repelling this objection is only by proving essentiality for the general and the specific: when the definition is opposed, or by proving accidentally for them or for one of them when the description is opposed. And this proof is very difficult in existing realities, as it has been said that distinguishing the essential from the accidental is extremely difficult to the point of impossibility, because the genus and the remote differentia and the common accident are similar. Likewise the proximate differentia and the property; so distinguishing them is extremely difficult. But since the matter is conjectural, demanding proof for it is not an impossible burden. As for linguistic and terminological concepts, it is easy for the one who is competent, because when a word is placed in language or in terminology, what is internal to that definition is essential, like the word, the placement, and the meaning, and what is external to it, like the entry of the lam, tanwin, and jar, is accidental. Hence Ibn al-Hajib said in the introduction to al-I'rab: 'And among its properties is the entry of the lam' etc. And since the objection from the side of the questioner is based on the definitiveness of the definition, and there are two conventions concerning the definition, he intended to point out the occurrence of the two conventions in it to remove confusion due to heedlessness of the difference between the two conventions according to the difference of the two sciences. And he indicated the possibility of answering the mentioned objection in another way, saying: 'And know that the definition being complete or incomplete — meaning the composite of essentials — whether the essential is a proximate or remote genus or a proximate differentia, it is only the convention of the people of the scale (logicians) and the convention of those who agree with them — i.e., the people of the scale. As for the definition in the convention of the people of the Arabic sciences, it is the definition that is comprehensive of its individuals and exclusive of its others, whether the definition is by essences (i.e., composed only of essentials) or by accidents (i.e., composed of accidents) or composed of both. So if the matter is as such, then the one who says 'define it as such' — i.e., the definer who claimed that this definition is a definition — can repel the mentioned objection that the questioner raised against the implicit claim, by [saying] that what is meant by 'definition' is the convention of the people of the Arabic sciences and fundamentals. For example, if one defines man as 'rational animal' and claims it is a definition, then he implicitly claims that 'animal' is genus and 'rational' is differentia, and that 'animal' is essential and 'rational' is essential. So the questioner may deny that 'animal' is genus and 'rational' is differentia: why may it not be a common accident or a necessary property? Then the definer may respond: According to the convention of the Arabic sciences, the definition is the comprehensive and exclusive definition, so as long as each part is internal to the definition, it is considered essential. So the definitions and descriptions of existing realities we call definitions and descriptions in reality, and definitions of linguistic and terminological concepts we call definitions and descriptions in name.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage deals with the difficulty of proving that the parts of a definition are genuinely essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi). The author presents a syllogism in the first figure to show that proving essentiality is difficult because it depends on distinguishing essential from accidental, which is itself difficult. The reason is that genus resembles common accident, and differentia resembles property, making them hard to tell apart in real essences. However, for conventional or linguistic concepts (like definitions in grammar), it is easy because the boundary is set by the practitioners of that art: whatever is included in the definition is considered essential, what is excluded is accidental. The author then explains that the objector's challenge can be answered by noting that 'definition' (had) has two different technical meanings: in the science of logic (mizan), it means a composite of essential parts only; in the Arabic sciences (like grammar and usul), it means any comprehensive and exclusive definition, regardless of whether its parts are essential or accidental. Therefore, if a definer claims his definition is a 'had' in the logical sense, he must prove essentiality, which is difficult. But if he means the broader sense (e.g., of Arabic grammar), then he can repel the objection by simply clarifying that he is using that convention. The author uses the example of defining man as 'rational animal': if the objector denies that 'animal' is genus, the definer can say that in the convention of Arabic sciences, as long as the definition is comprehensive and exclusive, it is acceptable. Thus, the passage illustrates how awareness of differing technical conventions (urf) can resolve definitional disputes.

Two Conventions of Hadd: Logical vs. Customary Definition
Author explains the two conventions of 'hadd' and how they resolve objections.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical definition (composed of essentials) and the customary definition (comprehensive and exclusive) and use this distinction to respond to objections.

Core Idea
A definition can be valid by convention even if its parts are not all essential.

The author reveals that the term 'hadd' (definition) carries two distinct technical meanings: one in logic (mizan) requiring essential parts only, and another in Arabic sciences (like grammar and usul al-fiqh) meaning any comprehensive and exclusive description. This lesson explains these conventions and shows how a definer can use them to repel objections, using the example of 'man is rational animal'.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two conventions of 'hadd'
First convention: the logicians' hadd, which must be composed solely of essential parts (dhatiyat). Second convention: the hadd of the Arabic sciences, which is any definition that is comprehensive of all individuals and exclusive of all non-individuals, regardless of whether its parts are essential or accidental.
2
See how the definer can respond to an objection
If an objector denies that a part is essential (e.g., 'animal' might be a common accident), the definer can say: 'I am using the convention of the Arabic sciences, where the definition is simply the comprehensive and exclusive expression. As long as the definition fits the definition, it is acceptable.' Thus the objection is deflected.
3
Apply to the example of 'rational animal'
Suppose someone defines 'man' as 'rational animal' and claims it is a hadd. The objector says: 'Animal might be a common accident, not a genus.' Under the logical convention, the definer must prove animal is essential. Under the Arabic sciences convention, he needs only to show that the definition is comprehensive and exclusive — which it is.
4
Understand the implication for definitional disputes
Many objections in definitional debates gloss over the fact that different sciences have different conventions. Recognizing this can resolve unnecessary disputes and allow definitions to be accepted on their own disciplinary terms.

مركبا أو مفردا ( أو بالعرضيات ( أى مركبا من العرضيات كذلك حقيقيا أو اسميا مركبا دائما أو مركبا أو مفرداً أو كان مركبا من الذاتيات والعرضيات فكلمة أو لمنع الخلو وإذا كان الأمر كذلك ) فلمن قال يحد بكذا ) أى فلصاحب التعريف الذى ادعى أن هذا التعريف حد ( أن يدفع المنع المذكور ( الذى أورده السائل على الدعوى الضمنية ) بأن المراد به ( أى بالحد (عرف أهل العلوم (العربية) والأصول مثلا إذا عرف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان الناطق وادعى أنه حد فكأنه ادعى أن الحيوان جنس والناطق فصل، وأن الحيوان ذاتى والناطق ذاتى فالسائل أن يمنع كون الحيوان جنسا والناطق فصلا لم لا يجوز أن يكون عرضا عاما أو خاصة لازمة فاصاحب التعريف المفهوم فما كان داخلا فيه فهو ذاتى وما كان خارجا عنه فهو عرضى له، وحدود الحقائق الموجودة ورسومها نسمها حدودا ورسوما بحسب الحقيقة وحدود المفهومات اللغوية والاصطلاحية حدودا ورسوما بحسب الاسم.
And repelling this mentioned objection is only by proving that each of the two or one of them is from the essential, or proving that each of the two or one of them is from the accidental. And this — i.e., proving essentiality and accidentally — is difficult or impossible, because it has been said that distinguishing the essential (whether genus or differentia) from the accidental (whether property or common accident) is difficult. And it is a proof in the first figure: This proof is difficult because it depends on distinguishing essential from accidental, and distinguishing essential from accidental is difficult; thus this proof depends on what is difficult, and what depends on what is difficult is difficult. The reason for its difficulty is that the genus resembles the common accident, and the differentia resembles the property, so distinguishing them is difficult, harder than cutting the [؟]... But this is only in existing realities and real definitions. As for conceptual matters, distinguishing them is easy because it is based on the conventions of the masters of the arts: what they consider as internal is essential, otherwise it is accidental. For example, if the grammarians define ... and the place of objection here is truly the implicit claim derived from the definer's saying 'they defined it' or 'they described it' etc. So the objection against it is not an objection against the definition, so the exception is not valid unless it is said that the definition's place is customary. And since the customary is what is considered, the exception was needed. Perhaps his saying 'so know' is an indication of this, but it has what it has. And repelling this objection is only by proving essentiality for the general and the specific: when the definition is opposed, or by proving accidentally for them or for one of them when the description is opposed. And this proof is very difficult in existing realities, as it has been said that distinguishing the essential from the accidental is extremely difficult to the point of impossibility, because the genus and the remote differentia and the common accident are similar. Likewise the proximate differentia and the property; so distinguishing them is extremely difficult. But since the matter is conjectural, demanding proof for it is not an impossible burden. As for linguistic and terminological concepts, it is easy for the one who is competent, because when a word is placed in language or in terminology, what is internal to that definition is essential, like the word, the placement, and the meaning, and what is external to it, like the entry of the lam, tanwin, and jar, is accidental. Hence Ibn al-Hajib said in the introduction to al-I'rab: 'And among its properties is the entry of the lam' etc. And since the objection from the side of the questioner is based on the definitiveness of the definition, and there are two conventions concerning the definition, he intended to point out the occurrence of the two conventions in it to remove confusion due to heedlessness of the difference between the two conventions according to the difference of the two sciences. And he indicated the possibility of answering the mentioned objection in another way, saying: 'And know that the definition being complete or incomplete — meaning the composite of essentials — whether the essential is a proximate or remote genus or a proximate differentia, it is only the convention of the people of the scale (logicians) and the convention of those who agree with them — i.e., the people of the scale. As for the definition in the convention of the people of the Arabic sciences, it is the definition that is comprehensive of its individuals and exclusive of its others, whether the definition is by essences (i.e., composed only of essentials) or by accidents (i.e., composed of accidents) or composed of both. So if the matter is as such, then the one who says 'define it as such' — i.e., the definer who claimed that this definition is a definition — can repel the mentioned objection that the questioner raised against the implicit claim, by [saying] that what is meant by 'definition' is the convention of the people of the Arabic sciences and fundamentals. For example, if one defines man as 'rational animal' and claims it is a definition, then he implicitly claims that 'animal' is genus and 'rational' is differentia, and that 'animal' is essential and 'rational' is essential. So the questioner may deny that 'animal' is genus and 'rational' is differentia: why may it not be a common accident or a necessary property? Then the definer may respond: According to the convention of the Arabic sciences, the definition is the comprehensive and exclusive definition, so as long as each part is internal to the definition, it is considered essential. So the definitions and descriptions of existing realities we call definitions and descriptions in reality, and definitions of linguistic and terminological concepts we call definitions and descriptions in name.
Source pages p. 30

Commentary
This passage deals with the difficulty of proving that the parts of a definition are genuinely essential (dhati) or accidental (aradi). The author presents a syllogism in the first figure to show that proving essentiality is difficult because it depends on distinguishing essential from accidental, which is itself difficult. The reason is that genus resembles common accident, and differentia resembles property, making them hard to tell apart in real essences. However, for conventional or linguistic concepts (like definitions in grammar), it is easy because the boundary is set by the practitioners of that art: whatever is included in the definition is considered essential, what is excluded is accidental. The author then explains that the objector's challenge can be answered by noting that 'definition' (had) has two different technical meanings: in the science of logic (mizan), it means a composite of essential parts only; in the Arabic sciences (like grammar and usul), it means any comprehensive and exclusive definition, regardless of whether its parts are essential or accidental. Therefore, if a definer claims his definition is a 'had' in the logical sense, he must prove essentiality, which is difficult. But if he means the broader sense (e.g., of Arabic grammar), then he can repel the objection by simply clarifying that he is using that convention. The author uses the example of defining man as 'rational animal': if the objector denies that 'animal' is genus, the definer can say that in the convention of Arabic sciences, as long as the definition is comprehensive and exclusive, it is acceptable. Thus, the passage illustrates how awareness of differing technical conventions (urf) can resolve definitional disputes.

The Two Terminologies of 'Hadd': Logic vs. Arabic Sciences
Author defines two technical senses of 'hadd'.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the logical definition of 'hadd' (composite of essential properties) and the Arabic-sciences definition (comprehensive and exclusive) and identify which one is being used in a given context.

Core Idea
Hadd is not one concept: in logic it demands essentials; in Arabic sciences it demands only comprehensiveness and exclusivity.

In this passage, the author alerts us to a common confusion: the term 'hadd' (definition) is used differently in logic and in the Arabic sciences. When you study definitions in fiqh, usul, or kalam, understanding this distinction is essential to avoid misunderstanding and to correctly defend or critique definitions. The author illustrates with the example of defining 'human' and shows how an objection can be deflected by clarifying the intended meaning of 'hadd'.

Guided Path
1
Locate the two definitions
In the Arabic, find where the author states the definition of 'hadd' according to the people of logic and according to the people of Arabic sciences.
2
Compare the requirements
Note that the logical definition requires composition from essential properties (dhatiyyat), while the Arabic definition only requires comprehensiveness (jami') and exclusivity (mani').
3
Apply the distinction
Take the example: defining human as 'rational animal'. If claimed as a logical hadd, it must be composed of genus (animal) and differentia (rational), both essential. If the objection is that 'animal' might not be the genus, the response can be that by 'hadd' we mean the Arabic definition, which doesn't require essential properties.

The word (kalima) is an utterance posited for a single meaning. So whatever is included in this definition is essential (dhati) like the utterance, the positing, and the meaning; and whatever is excluded from it, like the entry of lam, tanwin, and jar, is accidental ('aradi). Therefore, Ibn al-Hajib said in his introduction to grammar: 'Among its properties is the entry of lam,' etc. And since the objection (man') from the side of the questioner is based on the definitional status (haddiyya) of the definition, and there are two terminologies (istilahayn) concerning 'hadd', he intended to draw attention to the occurrence of the two terminologies in it to remove confusion due to forgetfulness about the difference between the two terminologies according to the difference between the two sciences. And he indicated the possibility of answering the mentioned objection by another way, saying: 'Know that the fact that the definition (hadd) is complete or incomplete (in the sense of composition) i.e., the composite (of essential properties) whether the essential is a proximate genus, or a remote genus, or a proximate differentia, is only the custom of the people of logic (ahl al-mizan) and those who agree with them.' As for the definition in the custom of the people of the Arabic sciences, it is the definition that is comprehensive for its individuals (he restricted it to that, and by its context he guarded against the more general and more specific definition) and exclusive (mani') of its others, whether the definition is by essential properties (i.e., composed of essential properties only, all or some, real or nominal, always or sometimes, composite or simple) or by accidental properties (i.e., composed of accidental properties similarly, real or nominal, always or sometimes, composite or simple) or composed of essential and accidental properties – the word 'or' is for preventing conjunction (man' al-khuluw). And when this is the case, then for the one who says 'it is defined by such-and-such' (i.e., the owner of the definition who claims that this definition is a hadd) he can repel the mentioned objection (which the questioner raised against the implicit claim) by saying that what is meant by 'hadd' is the custom of the people of the Arabic sciences and jurisprudence (usul). For example, if a human is defined as 'rational animal' and he claims that it is a hadd, then it is as if he claimed that animal is the genus and rational is the differentia, and that animal is essential and rational is essential; then the questioner may object (man') to animal being genus and rational being differentia: why may it not be a common accident or a necessary property? So the owner of the definition [responds] that the concept: whatever is included in it is essential and whatever is excluded is accidental for him. And the definitions of existing realities and their descriptions we call definitions and descriptions according to reality, and definitions of linguistic and conventional concepts are definitions and descriptions according to name. (Know that the fact that the definition is in the sense of composition) i.e., the definition composed of mere essential properties, is only the custom of the majority of the people of logic and those who agree with them. According to some investigators among the people of logic, it is the proximate differentia or the composite of pure essential properties. According to some precisians among them, it is the proximate differentia alone or composite with something else, whether that something else is essential or accidental. (As for in the custom of the people of Arabic) and jurisprudence, it is the comprehensive and exclusive definition. Perhaps the restriction to comprehensive and exclusive is to guard against definition by the more general or more specific. (Whether that definition is by pure essential properties or by pure accidental properties or by both. And often differences of terminologies occur, so that a definition by pure essential properties is prevented when claiming definitional status. So for the one who says 'it is defined by such-and-such' he can repel the mentioned objection (guarding against the objection based on lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, lack of clarity, or implication of absurdity by proving that that definition is a definition by definition) by saying that what is meant by 'hadd' here is the custom of the people of Arabic or jurisprudence or the custom of some precisians among the logicians. And if he objects to its composition, he repels by declaring that what I mean by it is the custom of the people of Arabic or jurisprudence or the custom of some investigators or some logicians. And if he objects to the existence of a proximate differentia, it is not repelled except by declaring that what I mean by it is the custom of the people of Arabic or jurisprudence. And since it is a place of question that 'man'' is a request for proof upon the premise and 'naqd' is invalidation of the proof by reasoning, it is not that he answers it by saying that what is meant by 'hadd' is the custom of the people of Arabic, which is the comprehensive and exclusive definition, and it is more general than the logical definition (?) and its (?). The summary is that your objection is directed at something the owner of the definition does not claim, because what is meant by 'hadd' is the custom of the people of Arabic, so it is repelled. And since it is a place that it might be said that 'man'' is a request for proof upon the premise of the proof and 'naqd' is invalidation of the proof by non-occurrence or by its implication of particular corruption, and there is no premise and no proof in the previous objections, he answered by saying: (then know that 'man'' which is the objection...).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'hadd' (definition) and outlines its two technical usages. The author begins by noting that the questioner's objection (man') is based on the definitional status of the definition. He points out that there are two terminologies (istilahayn) for 'hadd': one in logic (ahl al-mizan) and one in the Arabic sciences. In logic, a complete definition is composed of essential properties (dhatiyyat), whether genus or differentia. In the Arabic sciences, a definition is any description that is comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'), regardless of whether it uses essential or accidental properties. The passage then provides an example: defining 'human' as 'rational animal'. If one claims this is a logical hadd, the objection may be that 'animal' is not necessarily the genus, etc. The response is to clarify that by 'hadd' one means the Arabic sciences' definition, which does not require essential properties. Thus, the objection is deflected. The author then discusses various scenarios: if the objection is that the definition is not composed of essentials, the responder can say he intends the Arabic definition; if the objection is lack of a proximate differentia, again the Arabic definition suffices. Finally, he anticipates a further objection regarding the meaning of 'man' itself, which leads to the next section.

Responding to Objections by Clarifying the Meaning of Hadd
Author explains how to answer definitional objections by specifying the intended terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to analyze a dialectical exchange about definitions and determine how a respondent can deflect an objection by invoking the appropriate terminology of 'hadd'.

Core Idea
When an objection assumes one definition of 'hadd', specifying a different terminological custom dissolves the objection.

After establishing the two meanings of 'hadd', the author now explains how to use this distinction to answer objections (man'). When a questioner says 'this is not a complete definition', the responder needs to realize that the questioner might be using the logical sense, while the responder's intended sense is the Arabic-sciences sense. This lesson explores various scenarios and the correct response strategy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the objection
In the passage, find the type of objection: e.g., the definition lacks essential properties, or lacks a proximate differentia.
2
Determine the intended terminology
Based on the objection, infer which meaning of 'hadd' the questioner assumes.
3
Formulate the response
The responder can say 'What is meant by hadd here is the custom of the Arabic sciences (or jurisprudence, or some logicians)'.
4
Evaluate the strategy
Why does this response work? Because it shows the objection is irrelevant to the claim.

بأن المراد به ( أى بالحد (عرف أهل العلوم (العربية) والأصول مثلا إذا عرف الإنسان بأنه الحيوان الناطق وادعى أنه حد فكأنه ادعى أن الحيوان جنس والناطق فصل ، وأن الحيوان ذاتى والناطق ذاتى فالسائل أن يمنع ورسوما بحسب الحقيقة وحدود المفهومات اللغوية والاصطلاحية حدودا ورسوما بحسب الاسم (واعلم أن كون الحد بمعنى التركيب) أى بمعنى التعريف المتركب (عن) مجرد الذاتيات) إنما هو (عرف) جمهور أهل الميزان ومن وافقهم وعن بعض المحققين من أهل الميزان هو الفصل القريب أو المركب من الذاتيات المحضة ، وعند بعض المدققين منهم هو الفصل القريب وحده أو مركبا مع غيره سواء كان ذلك الغير ذاتيا أو عرضيا (وأما في عرف أهل العربية) والأصول فهو التعريف الجامع المانع) ولعل التقييد بالجامع والمانع احتراز عن التعريف بالأعم والأخص (سواء كان ذلك التعريف تعريفا (بالذاتيات) المحضة (أو) بالعرضيات) المحضة أو بهما وكثيرا ما يقع اختلاف الاصطلاحات فيمنع كون التعريف بالذاتيات المحضة عند الادعاء بالحدية ) فلمن قال يحد بكذا أن يدفع المنع المذكور ( احتراز عن المنع مستندا بعدم الجمع أو بعدم المنع أو جدم الجلاء أو باستلزام المحال بإثبات كون ذلك التعريف حداً بالتعريف ) بأن المراد به ( أى بالحد هنا عرف أهل العربية أو الأصول أو عرف بعض المدققين من الميزانيين. وأما إذا منع تركبه فيدفع بتحرير أن مرادى به عرف أهل العربية ) أو الأصول أو عرف بعض المحققين أو بعض من الميزانين وأما إذا منع وجود فصل قريب فلا يدفع إلا بتحرير أن مرادى به عرف أهل العربية أو الأصول ولما كان مظنة السؤال بأن المنع طلب الدليل على المقدمة والنقض إبطال الدليل بالاستدلال فلا أن يجيب عنه بأن المراد من الحد عرف أهل العربية وهو التعريف الجامع المانع وهو أعم من الحد اليزانى ورحمه . وحاصله أن منعك وارد على شيء لا يدعيه صاحب التعريف لأن المراد من الحد عرف أهل العربية فهو مدفوع . ولما كان مظنة أن يقال إن المنع طلب الدليل على مقدمة الدليل والنقض. إبطال الدليل بالتخلف أو باستلزامه بخصوص الفساد ولا مقدمة ولا دليل فى الاعتراضات ، السابقة أجاب عنه بقوله )
The word (kalima) is an utterance posited for a single meaning. So whatever is included in this definition is essential (dhati) like the utterance, the positing, and the meaning; and whatever is excluded from it, like the entry of lam, tanwin, and jar, is accidental ('aradi). Therefore, Ibn al-Hajib said in his introduction to grammar: 'Among its properties is the entry of lam,' etc. And since the objection (man') from the side of the questioner is based on the definitional status (haddiyya) of the definition, and there are two terminologies (istilahayn) concerning 'hadd', he intended to draw attention to the occurrence of the two terminologies in it to remove confusion due to forgetfulness about the difference between the two terminologies according to the difference between the two sciences. And he indicated the possibility of answering the mentioned objection by another way, saying: 'Know that the fact that the definition (hadd) is complete or incomplete (in the sense of composition) i.e., the composite (of essential properties) whether the essential is a proximate genus, or a remote genus, or a proximate differentia, is only the custom of the people of logic (ahl al-mizan) and those who agree with them.' As for the definition in the custom of the people of the Arabic sciences, it is the definition that is comprehensive for its individuals (he restricted it to that, and by its context he guarded against the more general and more specific definition) and exclusive (mani') of its others, whether the definition is by essential properties (i.e., composed of essential properties only, all or some, real or nominal, always or sometimes, composite or simple) or by accidental properties (i.e., composed of accidental properties similarly, real or nominal, always or sometimes, composite or simple) or composed of essential and accidental properties – the word 'or' is for preventing conjunction (man' al-khuluw). And when this is the case, then for the one who says 'it is defined by such-and-such' (i.e., the owner of the definition who claims that this definition is a hadd) he can repel the mentioned objection (which the questioner raised against the implicit claim) by saying that what is meant by 'hadd' is the custom of the people of the Arabic sciences and jurisprudence (usul). For example, if a human is defined as 'rational animal' and he claims that it is a hadd, then it is as if he claimed that animal is the genus and rational is the differentia, and that animal is essential and rational is essential; then the questioner may object (man') to animal being genus and rational being differentia: why may it not be a common accident or a necessary property? So the owner of the definition [responds] that the concept: whatever is included in it is essential and whatever is excluded is accidental for him. And the definitions of existing realities and their descriptions we call definitions and descriptions according to reality, and definitions of linguistic and conventional concepts are definitions and descriptions according to name. (Know that the fact that the definition is in the sense of composition) i.e., the definition composed of mere essential properties, is only the custom of the majority of the people of logic and those who agree with them. According to some investigators among the people of logic, it is the proximate differentia or the composite of pure essential properties. According to some precisians among them, it is the proximate differentia alone or composite with something else, whether that something else is essential or accidental. (As for in the custom of the people of Arabic) and jurisprudence, it is the comprehensive and exclusive definition. Perhaps the restriction to comprehensive and exclusive is to guard against definition by the more general or more specific. (Whether that definition is by pure essential properties or by pure accidental properties or by both. And often differences of terminologies occur, so that a definition by pure essential properties is prevented when claiming definitional status. So for the one who says 'it is defined by such-and-such' he can repel the mentioned objection (guarding against the objection based on lack of comprehensiveness, lack of exclusiveness, lack of clarity, or implication of absurdity by proving that that definition is a definition by definition) by saying that what is meant by 'hadd' here is the custom of the people of Arabic or jurisprudence or the custom of some precisians among the logicians. And if he objects to its composition, he repels by declaring that what I mean by it is the custom of the people of Arabic or jurisprudence or the custom of some investigators or some logicians. And if he objects to the existence of a proximate differentia, it is not repelled except by declaring that what I mean by it is the custom of the people of Arabic or jurisprudence. And since it is a place of question that 'man'' is a request for proof upon the premise and 'naqd' is invalidation of the proof by reasoning, it is not that he answers it by saying that what is meant by 'hadd' is the custom of the people of Arabic, which is the comprehensive and exclusive definition, and it is more general than the logical definition (?) and its (?). The summary is that your objection is directed at something the owner of the definition does not claim, because what is meant by 'hadd' is the custom of the people of Arabic, so it is repelled. And since it is a place that it might be said that 'man'' is a request for proof upon the premise of the proof and 'naqd' is invalidation of the proof by non-occurrence or by its implication of particular corruption, and there is no premise and no proof in the previous objections, he answered by saying: (then know that 'man'' which is the objection...).
Source pages p. 31

Commentary
This passage defines the term 'hadd' (definition) and outlines its two technical usages. The author begins by noting that the questioner's objection (man') is based on the definitional status of the definition. He points out that there are two terminologies (istilahayn) for 'hadd': one in logic (ahl al-mizan) and one in the Arabic sciences. In logic, a complete definition is composed of essential properties (dhatiyyat), whether genus or differentia. In the Arabic sciences, a definition is any description that is comprehensive (jami') and exclusive (mani'), regardless of whether it uses essential or accidental properties. The passage then provides an example: defining 'human' as 'rational animal'. If one claims this is a logical hadd, the objection may be that 'animal' is not necessarily the genus, etc. The response is to clarify that by 'hadd' one means the Arabic sciences' definition, which does not require essential properties. Thus, the objection is deflected. The author then discusses various scenarios: if the objection is that the definition is not composed of essentials, the responder can say he intends the Arabic definition; if the objection is lack of a proximate differentia, again the Arabic definition suffices. Finally, he anticipates a further objection regarding the meaning of 'man' itself, which leads to the next section.

When Can a Single Word Have Two Definitions?
Author enumerates the many possibilities when a definition is opposed by another definition.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether two definitions of the same term are permissible given the context and positing.

Core Idea
A word cannot have two complete definitions in one context unless by different positing.

In dialectic, when someone proposes a definition, an opponent may present a different definition. This lesson explains the logical rules governing when such opposing definitions are valid. We'll explore the distinctions between complete definitions (ḥadd tāmm) and incomplete descriptions (rasm), and the role of linguistic positing (waḍ‘).

Guided Path
1
Identify the two definitions
Determine whether each definition is a complete real definition (ḥadd tāmm) capturing essence, or an incomplete description (rasm nāqiṣ) using accidents.
Complete definitions are exclusive to the thing; incomplete ones may overlap.
2
Check the context (waḍ‘)
Are both definitions based on the same linguistic positing (the same assignment of meaning to the word)? If yes, they are in one waḍ‘; if not, multiple waḍ‘.
A single word can have multiple meanings by convention.
3
Apply the rule for complete definitions
If both are complete and in one waḍ‘, they cannot be different. The opponent's objection is valid because a thing cannot have two different essences.
Example: 'salah' cannot be both 'pillars with takbir' and 'spiritual connection' in the same usage.
4
Apply the exceptions
If at least one definition is incomplete (rasm), they can differ in generality. If based on different waḍ‘, they can differ even if both are complete.
Homonyms like 'ayn (eye, spring, coin) have multiple complete definitions by different waḍ‘.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فى بيان معارضة التعريف. للسائل أن عارضه بأن هذا معارض لذلك وكل شيء شأنه كذا فباطل، وبيانه أنه لا يكون للفظ واحد حقيقتان مختلفتان فلا يكون له حدان تاما بحسب الحقيقة وإن تساويا، وكذا لا يعرف لفظ واحد بتعريفين بحسب الحقيقة متباينين وإن كانا رسمين ناقصين وكذا لا يعرف لفظ واحد بتعريفين بحسب الحقيقة أحدهما أخص أو أعم من الآخر إلا إذا كان أحدهما أو كلاهما ناقصا. وأما إذا كان التعريفان أو أحدهما بحسب الاسم فيجوزان يكونا متباينين وكذا لا يجوز أن يكون أحدهما أخص أو أعم من الآخر إلا إذا كان أحدهما أو كلاهما ناقصا، هذا بالنسبة إلى موضع واحد. وأما بالنسبة إلى الأوضاع فيجوز تباينهما وإن كانا حدين تامين بحسب الحقيقة إذ يجوز أن يكون للفظ الواحد حقائق متباينة ومفهومات متباينات لتعدد وضعه فيجوز أن يكون للفظ واحد باعتبار وضعين تعريفات متباينات وإن كانا حدين تامين بحسب الحقيقة.
On the explanation of opposing a definition. It is permissible for the questioner to oppose it by saying that this definition is opposed by that definition, and everything whose case is such is false. Its explanation is that a single word does not have two different real meanings, so it does not have two complete definitions according to reality, even if they are equal. Likewise, a single word is not defined by two definitions according to reality that are different, even if they are incomplete (rasm). Likewise, a single word is not defined by two definitions according to reality, one of which is more specific or more general than the other, unless one or both are incomplete. As for when the two definitions or one of them are according to name (ism), it is permissible for them to be different. Likewise, it is not permissible for one to be more specific or more general than the other unless one or both are incomplete. This is with respect to a single context. As for with respect to multiple positing (awḍā‘), it is permissible for them to be different even if they are complete definitions according to reality, because it is permissible for a single word to have different real meanings and different concepts due to multiple positing. Thus it is permissible for a single word, by virtue of two positing, to have different definitions even if they are complete definitions according to reality.
Source pages p. 34

Commentary
This passage belongs to the topic of opposing definitions (mu‘āraḍat al-ta‘rīf) in dialectic. The author explains that a single word cannot have two different complete definitions (ḥadd tāmm) in the same usage context (waḍ‘ wāḥid). For example, one cannot define 'salah' both as 'a set of prescribed actions starting with takbir and ending with taslim' and 'a spiritual connection with Allah' in the same domain. If the two definitions are incomplete (rasm), they can differ in specificity or generality. However, if the definitions are based on different linguistic positing (e.g., homonyms), then it is permissible to have multiple complete definitions. This is a nuanced rule that helps a debater recognize when an opponent's objection is valid. The text later multiplies possibilities but the core insight is that a single word cannot have two different complete real definitions in the same setting.

Key Terms
معارضة التعريف
حد تام
رسم ناقص
وضع

In one waḍ‘, a word cannot have two different complete real definitions; exceptions are incomplete definitions or multiple waḍ‘.

Definition of Division and Its Two Types
Author defines division and explains its two types with terminology.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and conceptual division and identify the names of particulars based on the type of restrictions used.

Core Idea
Division is combining restrictions to produce distinct parts; its type depends on whether the division is real or conceptual.

In this passage, the author defines the logical operation of division (taqsīm) and distinguishes between real division (taqsīm ḥaqīqī) and conceptual division (taqsīm iʿtibārī). Understanding this distinction is crucial for precise reasoning in Islamic sciences, where definitions and classifications must be rigorous.

Guided Path
1
Extract the core definition
Find the phrase 'ضم قيود... لتحصيل أقسام'. Notice that the definition is common to both types but the adjectives for restrictions differ: 'متباينة' for real, 'متخالفة' for conceptual.
The verb 'ضم' implies combining restrictions onto the divided whole.
2
Differentiate real vs conceptual division
Real division uses 'قيود متباينة' (mutually exclusive restrictions) and produces 'أقسام متباينة' (mutually exclusive parts). Conceptual division uses 'قيود متخالفة' (diverse restrictions) and produces 'أقسام متمايزة بحسب العقل' (parts distinct only in the mind).
The difference in wording ('متباينة' vs 'متخالفة') is key: real division requires actual exclusivity, conceptual does not.
3
Identify terminology for particulars
The passage gives three names for the resulting divisions based on restriction type: if restrictions are essential (dhātiyyāt), they are called 'أنواع' (types); if accidental (ʿaraḍiyyāt), 'أصناف' (kinds); if both, 'أقسام' (divisions).
This naming is specific to real division; conceptual division does not have such sub-categories.
4
Address the condition of specificity
The author answers an implicit objection: why is the condition that each division is more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the divided whole not mentioned? He states that in real division this condition holds, but in conceptual division it may not; however, it is understood from the context of 'mutually exclusive restrictions'.
This shows the author's economy: he omits what is implied.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل: في بيان تعريف تقسيم الكلى إلى جزئياته. قال: ومعناه: ضم قيود متباينة لتحصيل أقسام متباينة. وذلك في التقسيم الحقيقي، سواء كان ذلك بالذاتيات أو بالعرضيات أو بكليهما. والجزئيات بحسب الأول تسمى أنواعًا، وبالثاني أصنافًا، وبالثالث أقسامًا. وأما التقسيم الاعتباري فهو ضم قيود متخالفة لتحصيل أقسام متمايزة بحسب العقل. ثم قال: إن قلت: إن من شرائط تقسيم الكلى إلى جزئياته أخصية القسم من المتقسم، فلم لم يذكره؟ قلت: شرطه أن يكون كل قسم منه أخص من المقسم بحسب التعقل، وقد يكون مساويًا بحسب الجمل في التقسيم الاعتباري منه، لكن لما استفيد من المنع تركه.
Chapter: On explaining the definition of dividing the universal into its particulars. He said: Its meaning is: combining mutually exclusive (or diverse) restrictions to obtain mutually exclusive (or distinct) divisions. That is in real division, whether it is by essential properties or accidental properties or both. The particulars according to the first are called types (anwa‘), according to the second are called kinds (asnaf), and according to the third are called divisions (aqsam). As for conceptual division, it is combining diverse restrictions to obtain divisions distinct according to the intellect. Then he said: If you say: Among the conditions of dividing the universal into its particulars is that the division is more specific than the divided whole, so why did he not mention it? I say: Its condition is that each division is more specific than the divided whole in terms of conceptualization, and it may be equal in terms of generality in conceptual division thereof, but because it is understood from the prohibition he omitted it.
Source pages p. 38

Commentary
This passage provides the formal definition of division (taqsīm) in logic. The author states that division means 'combining mutually exclusive (or diverse) restrictions to obtain mutually exclusive (or distinct) divisions'. This definition applies to both real and conceptual division, but the wording is tailored to real division first. In real division, the restrictions (quyūd) can be essential (dhātiyyāt) or accidental (ʿaraḍiyyāt) or both. The resulting particulars are named differently based on the type of restrictions: if the restrictions are essential, the particulars are called 'types' (anwā‘); if accidental, they are called 'kinds' (aṣnāf); if both, they are simply called 'divisions' (aqsām). For conceptual division, the definition is slightly different: 'combining diverse restrictions (quyūd mutakhālifa) to obtain divisions distinct according to the intellect'. The difference in wording (متخالفة vs متباينة) indicates that conceptual division does not require real exclusivity, only conceptual distinctness. The passage then addresses a potential objection: why does the definition not mention that the division must be more specific (akhaṣṣ) than the divided whole? The author answers that in real division this condition is necessary, but in conceptual division the division may be equal in generality; however, since the condition is understood from the context of 'mutually exclusive restrictions', he omitted it. This shows the author's precision in defining logical terms.

Key Terms
ضم قيود
الذاتيات
العرضيات
أخصية القسم من المقسم

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) Divisions (أقسام)
D) Species (أجناس)

Key Takeaway
Division is defined as combining restrictions to obtain distinct parts; real division yields mutually exclusive parts, while conceptual division yields parts distinct only in the mind.

Fixing a Division When Parts Are More General
Author explains the objection and answer when division parts are more general

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify an invalid division where a part is more general than the divided term and reformulate it correctly using tahrir al-taqseem.

Core Idea
A valid division requires parts more specific than the divided term.

In Islamic logic, dividing a term into categories is a common method for analysis. But a division can be flawed if a part is broader than the term being divided. For example, saying 'human is either white or black' fails because 'white' applies to many non-humans. This lesson shows you how to spot such errors and correct them by refining the division.

Guided Path
1
Identify the divided term (maqsamm) and its parts (aqsam)
Locate the term being divided (e.g., 'human') and the parts listed (e.g., 'white' and 'black'). The division statement usually has the form 'X is either Y or Z'.
2
Check each part against the divided term
Ask: Is this part more general (a'am) than the divided term? That is, does it apply to things that are not instances of the divided term? If yes, the part is more general.
3
Declare the division invalid if any part is more general
A valid division requires that each part is a subtype of the divided term. A part that is more general includes non-instances and breaks the exclusivity of the division.
This is the naqd (objection).
4
Apply tahrir al-taqseem (reformulation) to fix the division
Incorporate the divided term into each part, creating a new division where the parts are now qualified. For example, instead of 'human is either white or black', say 'human is either white human or black human'. This ensures parts are more specific.
This can be done by refining the part, the divided term, or both.
5
Verify the reformulated division is valid
Ensure that the new parts are indeed subtypes of the divided term and that they are mutually exclusive and exhaustive (if intended). In the example, 'white human' is a subtype of 'human', so valid.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما إذا قلت في تقسيم الإنسان إلى قسمين الإنسان إما أبيض أو أسود. وحاصل النقض أن هذا التقسيم متضمن للباطل لكون القسم فيه أعم من المقسم. فيجاب عنه بمنع الصغرى بأن المقسم معتبر في الأقسام. وقد يستند بتحرير القسم أو المقسم أو كليهما. ولا مجال لمنع الكبرى. وقد يجاب بالنقضين أيضا وتغيير التقسيم. قيل ربما ينقض عند كونه أعم مطلقا من القسم بأن هذا التقسيم باطل لأنه يلزم فيه انقسام الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره كما إذا قلت الحيوان إما إنسان أو نام. فيجاب عن هذا النقض بمنع الصغرى بتحرير القسم أو المقسم أو كليهما لكن يلزم تحرير التقسيم بأنه اعتباري عند تحرير المقسم بأن المقسم معتبر في الأقسام. أقول فيه أنه لا اختصاص لهذا التصوير بكون التقسيم أعم مطلقا بل يجرى في الأعم من وجه كما لا يخفى.
Such as when you say in the division of human into two parts: human is either white or black. The summary of the objection is that this division includes falsehood because the part in it is more general than the divided term. The answer to it is by preventing the minor premise, by [saying] that the divided term is considered within the parts. And it may be supported by redefining the part or the divided term or both. And there is no room for preventing the major premise. And it may also be answered with both objections and changing the division. It was said: perhaps it is objected when it is absolutely more general than the part, that this division is invalid because it necessitates the division of the thing into itself and another, as when you say the animal is either human or ostrich? So the answer to this objection is by preventing the minor premise by redefining the part or the divided term or both, but it necessitates redefining the division as being mental (i'tibari) when redefining the divided term as that the divided term is considered within the parts. I say: this formulation is not specific to the division being absolutely more general, but rather it applies to the case of general in one way, as is not hidden.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage deals with an objection to a division when one of the parts (qism) is more general (a'am) than the divided term (maqsamm). The first example is dividing 'human' into 'white' and 'black' – here 'white' and 'black' are more general than 'human', because they apply to non-humans too. The objection is that such a division is false (batil). The answer is to prevent the minor premise by explaining that the divided term is implicitly understood in the parts: the real parts are 'white human' and 'black human', not just 'white' and 'black'. This is called tahrir al-taqseem (refinement of the division). The major premise that whatever has a part more general than the divided term is false is not denied. The passage then considers the case where the part is absolutely more general (a'am mutlaq) than the divided term, like dividing 'animal' into 'human' and 'ostrich'? (nam? likely an animal). This would imply dividing a thing into itself and another (since 'human' is a subset of 'animal', 'human' and 'ostrich' are both animals). The answer is again to refine the parts or the divided term, but here the division is purely mental (i'tibari): the divided term is considered within the parts in a conceptual sense. The commentator notes that this issue is not limited to absolutely more general but also applies to general in one respect (a'am min wajh). The key lesson is that a valid division must have parts that are more specific (akhass) than the divided term, otherwise the division is flawed.

Key Terms
مقسم
قسم
نقض
تحرير التقسيم

A) The parts are not exhaustive (there are other colors).
B) The parts are more general than the divided term.
C) The parts are overlapping.
D) The division is perfectly valid.

Key Takeaway
For a division to be valid, each part must be more specific (akhass) than the divided term; if a part is more general, the division is invalid and must be refined by incorporating the divided term into the part (tahrir al-taqseem).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

Identify the flaw in the division: 'Salah is either obligatory or voluntary.' Are the parts more general than 'salah'? (Answer: 'obligatory' and 'voluntary' are types of salah, so more specific — valid. Now change to: 'Salah is either fard or dhikr.' Here 'dhikr' is more general than salah (includes Quran reading, tasbih). Correct it using tahrir al-taqseem.

But if the division were 'Contract is either agreement or transfer', 'agreement' is more general — invalid. Reformulate as 'Contract is either sale agreement or gift agreement'.)
