The Condition of Specificity in Logical Division
Author explains the objection and answer when a part equals the divided term in extension

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a part equals the divided term in extension, explain why such a division is problematic, and apply the answer based on mental conception to defend a valid division.

Core Idea
A part equal in extension to the divided term destroys the division unless saved by conceptual specificity.

In the previous lesson, we learned that a valid division requires conditions like exhaustiveness and mutual exclusivity. Here we encounter a more subtle condition: each part must be more specific (akhas) than the divided term. When a part equals the divided term in extension, the division becomes a self-division – but the logicians offer a nuanced defense based on the mental nature of division.

Guided Path
1
Recall the second condition of division
The second condition is that each part must be more specific (akhas) than the divided term. Find its statement in the passage: 'الشرط الثاني' refers to this. Why is this condition necessary?
Without it, the division reduces to dividing a thing into itself and another.
2
Identify the objection when a part equals the divided term
The passage explains that an objection occurs when 'بعض الأقسام المذكورة في التقسيم مساويا للقسم' (some parts are equal to the divided term). Understand 'مساواة' as coextensiveness: the part applies to exactly the same individuals as the divided term.
Equality can be by synonymy (same concept) or conventional equality (different concepts, same extension).
3
Analyze the two examples given
First example: dividing human (إنسان) into bashar (بشر) and zangi (زنجي) – here bashar is synonymous with human, so equality is clear. Second example: dividing human into muta'ajjib (متعجب) and zangi – these are coextensive but conceptually distinct (muta'ajjib = rational animal, zangi = black human). Both are equal in extension to human.
Note that the division fails in both cases according to the objection.
4
Examine the answer: preventing the premise by redefining the division as mental
The text offers an answer: 'بمنع الكبرى في صورة المساواة مستندا بتحرير التقسيم بأن القسمة اعتبارية' – prevent the major premise by clarifying that the division is mental (i'tibariyya). Even if the part equals the divided term in predication (extension), it is more specific in conception (ta'aqqul). Thus, the part is not identical in meaning, and the division is saved.
This answer preserves the logical validity of divisions where parts are coextensive but conceptually distinct.
5
Connect to the third and fourth conditions
The author notes that this objection actually concerns the fourth condition (the part is more specific) and possibly the third condition (mutual exclusivity), because synonyms are not mutually exclusive. Reflect on how these conditions interrelate.
This shows the layered nature of logical conditions in division.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقد ينقض تقسيم الكلى إلى جزئياته بانتفاء الشرط الثاني بأنه باطل لأنه تقسيم الشيء إلى نفسه وإلى غيره. وذلك النقض واقع إذا كان بعض الأقسام المذكورة في التقسيم مساويا للقسم. والمراد من المساواة هنا اتحاد الشيئين فيما يصدق عليه سواء كانا متحدين مفهوما فيكونان مترادفين أولا فيكونان متساويين اصطلاحا. مثال الأول كتقسيم الانسان إلى البشر والزنجي. ومثال الثاني كتقسيمه إلى المتعجب والزنجي. ويجاب عنه بمنع الصغرى مستندا بتحرير القسم أو المقسم أو كليهما أو تغيير التقسيم كلا أو بعضا. وبمنع الكبرى في صورة المساواة مستندا بتحرير التقسيم بأن القسمة اعتبارية، والمقسم معتبر في الأقسام فيكون القسم أخص بحسب التعقل وإن كان مساويا بحسب الحمل. ولما لم يوجد كون القسم نفس المقسم لم يتعرض له قبل هذا نقض بانتفاء الشرط الرابع المستفاد من المنع وهو كون القسم أخص من المقسم على ما أشير إليه. أقول وعكن أن يكون بانتفاء الشرط الثالث، وهو تباين الأقسام لأن البشر لايباين الزنجي، فلا تباين بينهما تأمل في هذا المقام فانه من مزالق الأقدام؛ والله أعلم
And it may be objected to the division of the universal into its particulars by the absence of the second condition, that it is invalid because it is a division of the thing into itself and another. And that objection occurs when some of the parts mentioned in the division are equal to the divided term. What is meant by equality here is the two things being coextensive, whether they are identical in concept (synonyms) or not (conventionally equal). Example of the first: dividing human into bashar and zangi. Example of the second: dividing him into muta'ajjib and zangi. And it is answered by preventing the minor premise, supported by redefining the part or the divided term or both, or changing the division entirely or partially. And by preventing the major premise in the case of equality, supported by redefining the division – that the division is mental (i'tibari), and the divided term is considered within the parts, so the part is more specific in conception (bi-hasab al-ta'aqqul) even if it is equal in predication (bi-hasab al-haml). And since the case of the part being exactly the divided term does not occur, it was not addressed before. This is an objection by the absence of the fourth condition derived from the condition of prevention, which is that the part is more specific than the divided term, as was indicated. I say: it could be that it is by the absence of the third condition, which is the mutual exclusivity of parts, because bashar and zangi are not mutually exclusive, so there is no exclusivity between them. Ponder this place, for it is a slippery slope; and Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 51

Commentary
This passage discusses an objection to a division when one of the parts is equal (musawi) in extension to the divided term, divided into two subcases: synonymy (e.g., 'bashar' and 'zangi' both mean human) and conventional equality (e.g., 'muta'ajjib – rational laughing animal' and 'zangi – black human', both coextensive with human). Such a division is considered invalid because it divides a thing into itself and another (since one part is identical in extension to the divided term). The answer often involves preventing the minor premise by redefining the division – either by redefining the part or the divided term, or by changing the division. Sometimes the major premise (that such a division is invalid) is prevented by arguing that the division is mental (i'tibari): the part is not identical to the divided term in conception (ta'aqqul), though it is coextensive in predication (haml). This effectively saves the division from being a self-division. The author then notes that this objection actually concerns the absence of the fourth condition (the part being more specific), but he also points out that it could involve the third condition (mutual exclusivity), since synonyms are not mutually exclusive. The passage ends with a warning that this is a subtle point.

C) Because such divisions are never actually used.
D) Because the third condition of mutual exclusivity overrides the problem.

Key Takeaway
A part equal in extension to the divided term does not necessarily invalidate the division if the part is conceptually more specific, because division is a mental act.

The Condition of Mutual Exclusivity in Division
Author explains objection based on overlapping parts (tasadaq) and the requirement of mutual exclusivity

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify overlapping parts (tasadaq) in a division and determine whether the division violates the condition of mutual exclusivity.

Core Idea
A valid division requires parts to be mutually exclusive; overlapping parts defeat its purpose.

In classical Islamic logic, a valid division must satisfy two key conditions: the parts must be exhaustive (collectively covering the divided term) and mutually exclusive (not overlapping). This lesson focuses on the second condition: mutual exclusivity, or tabayun al-aqsam. The passage introduces an objection to a division when the parts overlap, using the term 'tasadaq' to describe co-application to the same individual. Understanding this condition is crucial for constructing sound definitions and classifications in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parts of the division
Locate the divided term (al-muqassam) and the parts (al-aqsam) into which it is divided. For example, in 'animal is either human or white', the parts are 'human' and 'white'.
2
Check for overlap between any two parts
Determine if any two parts can apply to the same individual simultaneously. If they do, there is tasadaq. Use the logical relation 'umum min wajh' (generality in one respect) as a sign of overlap.
Overlap means the parts are not mutually exclusive.
3
Assess the validity of the division
If any overlap exists, the division is invalid (fasad) because it fails to achieve the purpose of division: differentiation (tamayyuz) between the parts. A valid division must have parts that are distinct and non-overlapping.
The passage states: 'every division containing overlap of parts is corrupt.'
4
Apply the condition to Islamic science examples
Practice by examining divisions commonly used in fiqh or usul al-fiqh. For instance, dividing 'obligatory acts' into 'daily prayers' and 'fasting' is valid (no overlap), but dividing 'acts of worship' into 'prayer' and 'prostration' would be invalid because prostration is part of prayer.
Ensure the parts are both exhaustive and exclusive.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في بيان الاعتراض على التقسيم بانتفاء الشرط الثالث، وهو تباين الأقسام مطلقا. قد ينقض التقسيم مطلقا، سواء كان تقسيم الكلى إلى جزئياته أو تقسيم الكل إلى الأجزاء، بسبب أن يقول إن فيه تصادق الأقسام كلا أو بعضا، أي صدقها على شيء واحد. ولعل فائدة التفسير أن المراد من التفاعل هنا اشتراك الأقسام في أصل الفعل من غير قصد إلى أن الفاعل فعل بالآخر ما فعل الآخر به صريحا أو ضمنا على ما هو المشهور في باب المفاعلة. قال المحقق التفتازاني في شرح تصريف الزنجاني في بيان الفرق بين فاعل وتفاعل: وذلك لأن وضع فاعل لنسبة الفعل إلى الفاعل للتعلق بغيره مع أن الغير أيضا فعل ذلك، وتفاعل وضعه لنسبته إلى المشتركين فيه من غير قصد إلى تعلقه له. وحاصل النقض أن هذا التقسيم فاسد لأن هذا التقسيم فيه تصادق الأقسام وكل ما شأنه كذلك فهو فاسد. وذلك إذا كان بين الأقسام كلها أو بعضها عموم من وجه. مثال الأول كما إذا قلنا في تقسيم الحيوان: الحيوان إما إنسان وإما أبيض، وبينهما عموم من وجه لأنهما يصدقان على الإنسان الأبيض، ويفترق الأول بدون الثاني في الإنسان الأسود، والثاني بدون الأول في الفرس الأبيض. ومثال الثاني كما إذا قلنا: الإنسان إما رومي أو حبشي أو أبيض. قال القطب الرازي في شرح المطالع: المقصود من التقسيم التمايز بين الأقسام، والغرض منه إيراد دليل الكبرى للدليل السابق أي فكل ما فيه تصادق الأقسام فهو فاسد، وإلا لما كان المقصود من التقسيم التمايز بين الأقسام.
Chapter on explaining the objection to division by the absence of the third condition, which is the mutual exclusivity of parts absolutely. The division may be objected to absolutely, whether it is the division of a universal into its particulars or the division of a whole into its parts, by saying: it contains overlap (tasadaq) of the parts, all or some, i.e., they apply to one thing. Perhaps the benefit of this interpretation is that what is meant by interaction (tafa'ul) here is the sharing of the parts in the root action without intending that one agent did to the other what the other did to it, explicitly or implicitly, according to what is well-known in the chapter of mutual action. Al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani said in his commentary on Tasrif al-Zanjani, explaining the difference between fa'ala and tafa'ala: 'This is because fa'ala is placed for attributing the action to the agent in relation to something else, while the other also did that action, and tafa'ala is placed for attributing it to the participants without intending attachment to it.' The summary of the objection is that this division is corrupt because it contains overlap of the parts, and whatever is so is corrupt. That occurs when between the parts, all or some, there is generality in one respect (umum min wajh). Example of the first: as when we say in the division of animal: animal is either human or white, and between them there is generality in one respect because they both apply to the white human, and the first without the second is distinguished by the black human, and the second without the first by the white horse. Example of the second: as when we say: human is either Roman or Abyssinian or white. Al-Qutb al-Razi said in his commentary on al-Matali': 'The purpose of division is differentiation (tamayyuz) between the parts.' And the intention is to present evidence for the major premise of the previous argument, i.e., every division containing overlap of parts is corrupt; otherwise the purpose of division would not be differentiation between the parts.
Source pages p. 52

Commentary
This passage introduces a new chapter on the objection to a division based on the absence of the third condition: mutual exclusivity of parts (tabayun al-aqsam). The objection is that the parts overlap (tasadaq), meaning they apply to the same thing. The type of overlap discussed is 'generality in one respect' (umum min wajh), where two terms intersect in some individuals but each has unique ones. Two examples are given: (1) dividing 'animal' into 'human' and 'white' – these overlap on white humans; (2) dividing 'human' into 'Roman', 'Abyssinian', and 'white' – the last overlaps with the first two. The text quotes al-Taftazani on the linguistic nuance of tafa'ul (interaction) used in 'tasadaq', and al-Qutb al-Razi affirming that the purpose of division is to differentiate the parts. Therefore, any division where parts overlap fails to achieve this purpose and is invalid. This condition is essential for a valid division in classical logic.

Key Terms
تصادق
عموم من وجه
تماييز
تفاعل

Key Takeaway
A division is valid only if its parts are mutually exclusive; overlapping parts (tasadaq) in any form render the division corrupt because they prevent the differentiation intended by division.

Real vs Conceptual Division and the Role of Overlap
Author defines real and conceptual division with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between real and conceptual division by checking whether the parts are distinct in reality or only in the mind, and identify the impact of overlap (tasadaq) on each.

Core Idea
Conceptual division remains valid even when the parts overlap in reality; only real division requires external separation.

In logic, dividing a concept into its parts can be done in two fundamentally different ways: real division (taqsim haqiqi) requires the parts to be actually separate in the external world, while conceptual division (taqsim i'tibari) only requires mental distinctness. This passage from the Sharh al-Waldiyya clarifies this, addressing a common confusion about overlap (tasadaq). The author uses the five universals as a powerful example to show how one thing can be all five parts conceptually, even though in reality it is a single entity.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two types of division.
Read the passage and locate the definitions of 'real division' and 'conceptual division'. Real division makes the divided into parts distinct in reality (fi al-waqi'), while conceptual division divides a universal into concepts distinct only in the mind (fi al-'aql). The author gives examples: dividing 'thing' into 'existent' and 'non-existent' for real division, and dividing the universal into its five parts for conceptual division.
2
Understand the role of tasadaq (overlap).
The passage introduces a potential objection: if the parts overlap (apply to the same thing), doesn't that invalidate division? The author clarifies that overlap only invalidates real division, because real division requires real separation. Conceptual division is not harmed by overlap, because the parts are only conceptually distinct. The key phrase: 'Overlap absolutely only invalidates real division'.
Overlap (tasadaq) means two or more terms applying to the same individual.
3
Examine the example of the five universals.
The five universals (genus, species, differentia, property, common accident) are conceptually distinct, yet they can all apply to one thing like 'colored' (mulawwan). Al-Fanari explains how 'colored' can play each role depending on the relation. This illustrates that conceptual division does not require real separation; only conceptual distinctness suffices.
The same thing can be a genus for one thing, a species for another, etc., without contradiction.
4
Apply the distinction to a new example.
Practice by taking a concept like 'living being' and dividing it into 'plant' and 'animal'. Is this real or conceptual? If we consider them as categories that are actually distinct in the world (no organism is both plant and animal), it is real division. If we consider them as conceptual subclasses that may overlap in borderline cases, it might be conceptual. The criterion: check whether the parts are mutually exclusive in external existence.
Real division parts cannot coexist in the same individual; conceptual division parts can.

التمايز فى الواقع قال ( أقول : يعنى) أى يقصد القطب ) من التمايز التباين بين جميع الأقسام مطلقا أعم من أن يكون في الواقع أو في العقل . ولما كان هنا مظنة توهم أن التصادق ينافي التباين مطلقا مع أنه غير مضر للتقسيم الاعتبارى دفعه بقوله ( لكن التصادق ) مطلقا ) إنما يبطل به ) أى بالتصادق ( التقسيم الحقيقى سواء كان تقسيم الكلى إلى جزئياته أو تقسيم الكل إلى أجزائه وسواء كان عقليا أو استقرائيا (وهو) أى التقسيم الحقيقى (جعل المقسم) كليات أو كلا ( أشياء) جزئيات أو أجزاء ) متمايزة ) متباينة ( فى الواقع ( كتقسيم الشيء إلى الموجود والمعدوم ( ولا يضر ) التصادق ( التقسيم الاعتبارى ) وهو مختص بتقسيم الكلى إلى جزئياته مطلقا ( وهو ) أى التقسيم الاعتبارى ) تقسيم الكلى ) مطلقا ) إلى مفهومات ( أى جزئيات مطلقة ( متباينة ) كلا ( فى العقل ( لا فى الواقع سواء كان بعضها متباينا في الواقع أيضا أولا ( وإن كانت ( الأقسام ) متصادقة في الواقع ( كلا أو بعضا في جميع الأفراد لا مطلقا حق يقال نقيض الموصول لا بد أن يكون أخرى بالحكم مع أنه ليس كذلك ) . كتقسيم ( الميزانيين ( الكلى إلى أقسامه الخمسة ) وهى الجنس والنوع والفصل والخاصة والعرض العام ومفهوماتها في كتب المنطق (مع أنها ) أى الأقسام ) متصادقة في الملون كما بينه الفنارى ( أى على ما بينه أو مثل ما بينه حيث قال يمكن أن ( يكون شيء واحد جنسا ونوعا وفصلا وخاصة وعرضا عاما كالملون فانه جنس للأسود أى أعم منه فان الملون يعم الأبيض وغيره ونوع للمكيف : أى أخص منه فان المكيف يعم الحار والبارد وغير الملون كالهواء ، وفصل للكثيف : أى للجسم الكثيف إذ تعريفه جسم ماون وخاصة للجسم فان ما ليس بجسم بأن يكون جوهرا مجردا كالنفس الانسانية على ما زعمه البعض فلا يمكن أن يكون ملونا ثم لا يلزم من أن يكون الملون خاصة للجسم أن يتصف جميع أفراده باللون
Regarding distinction in reality, he said (I mean: the Qutb) that distinction means separation among all parts absolutely, whether in reality or in the mind. Since there was a suspicion that overlap contradicts distinction absolutely, while it does not harm conceptual division, he repelled it by saying: 'But overlap absolutely only invalidates real division, whether the division of a universal into its particulars or the division of a whole into its parts, whether intellectual or inductive. Real division is making the divided (universals or whole) into things (particulars or parts) that are distinct (separate) in reality, like dividing a thing into existent and non-existent. Overlap does not harm conceptual division, which is specific to dividing a universal into its particulars absolutely. Conceptual division is dividing the universal absolutely into concepts (i.e., absolute particulars) that are distinct (all) in the mind, not in reality, whether some of them are distinct in reality as well or not, even if the parts are overlapping in reality (all or some in all individuals, not absolutely, such that the opposite of the relative must be more deserving of the judgment, but it is not like that). Like the logicians' division of the universal into its five parts, which are genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident, and their concepts in logic books, even though they overlap in 'colored' as al-Fanari explained, where he said: 'One thing can be genus, species, differentia, property, and common accident, like colored: it is genus for black (more general, covering white and others), species for qualified (more specific, covering hot, cold, and non-colored like air), differentia for dense (i.e., dense body, defined as a colored body), property for body (since what is not a body, like a separate substance as some claim the human soul, cannot be colored), and it does not follow from colored being a property of body that all its individuals are colored.
Source pages p. 53

Commentary
This passage defines two types of division: real (haqiqi) and conceptual (i'tibari). The author first clarifies that distinction (tamayyuz) means separation among the parts of a division, whether that separation occurs in external reality (fi al-waqi') or only in the mind (fi al-'aql). He then anticipates a potential misunderstanding: one might think that overlap (tasadaq) among the parts—i.e., that they apply to the same thing—would invalidate any division. The author responds that overlap only invalidates real division, not conceptual division. Real division is when the divided (al-muqassam) is made into parts that are distinct in external reality, such as dividing 'thing' into 'existent' and 'non-existent'. Conceptual division, on the other hand, is specifically the division of a universal into its particulars, and it only requires that the parts are conceptually distinct (mutamayyiza fi al-'aql), even if they overlap in reality. The author gives a key example: the logicians' division of the universal into its five parts (genus, species, differentia, property, common accident). These five are conceptually distinct but can all apply to one thing like 'colored' (mulawwan). Al-Fanari explains how colored can be a genus for black, a species for qualified, a differentia for dense body, a property for body, and a common accident for animal. This example illustrates that conceptual division remains valid despite real overlap.

تقسيم حقيقى
تقسيم اعتبارى
تصادق
تمایز
مفهوم

D) Dividing colors into black and white (real division)

Key Takeaway
Conceptual division only requires conceptual distinctness, not real separation, making it valid even when the parts overlap in reality, as seen in the five universals example.

The 28 Relations of Majaz
Author enumerates and categorizes the types of relations (alaqat) used in majaz.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the 28 types of relations (alaqat) in majaz and classify new examples into the correct relation type.

Core Idea
Majaz relies on 28 necessary relations between literal and figurative meaning.

In this passage, the author systematically enumerates the 28 relations that justify figurative usage in Arabic. Understanding these relations is essential for analyzing metaphors in the Quran, Hadith, and legal texts. We will walk through each type with examples.

Guided Path
1
Identify the figurative expression (majaz)
Locate the phrase that is used non-literally. For example, 'lion' for a brave man.
This is the starting point.
2
Determine literal and intended meanings
What does the word originally denote? What is actually meant? For 'lion', literal: animal; intended: brave man.
3
Identify the type of relation (alaqa)
Match the connection to one of the 28 types from the list: similarity, causation, part-whole, etc.
Use the examples in the passage as a guide.
4
Verify the relation is valid
Ensure the relation is recognized in Arabic usage, not arbitrary. The author notes a restriction: one cannot take 'horse' from 'book' without a valid relation.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
العلاقة تعتبر كلية وهي لزوم المعنى للمستعمل فيه للموضوع له في الجملة، ولذا قبل المجاز فيه انتقال من الملزوم إلى اللازم والكناية بالعكس. واعلم بأن اللازم مالم يكن ماروما لم ينتقل منه فهما مشتركان في الانتقال من الملزوم إلى اللازم وجزئية فهي إما مشابهة كالأسد في الرجل الشجاع وإما مصدرية كاليد في النعمة أو مظهرية كما في ويد الله فوق أيديهم إذ المراد بها القدرة لظهور الأثر فيها أو مجاورة كالراوية في المزادة أو جزئية كالعين في الرقيب أو كلية كالأصابع في نحو يجعلون أصابعهم في آذانهم أو أوسبية نحو أمطرت السماء نباتا، أو كون نحو وآتوا اليتامى أموالهم، أو أول نحو إني أراني أعصر خمرا، أو محلية نحو واسأل القرية أو حالية نحو في رحمة الله أو آلية نحو واجعل لي لسان صدق أي ذكرا. أو إطلاق أو تقيد أو محموم كالدابة في الفرس، أو خصوص كالفرس في الدابة، أو قوة كالمسكر في الخمر، أو لازمية أو ملزومية نحو أديت زيدا في ضربته وضربته فأدته أو علية أو معلولية كالنار في الحرارة والحرارة في النار، أو تعلق كالضرب في الضارب وبالعكس، أو شرطية كالإيمان في الصلاة أو مشروطية كعكسها، أو دلالية أو مدلولية، فمجموع العلاقات ثمانية وعشرون. وقال المحقق التفتازاني: وأنواع العلاقات المعتبرة كثيرة ترتقى على ما ذكروه إلى خمسة وعشرين، وقد ضبطها صاحب التوضيح في تسعة: الكون والأول والاستعداد والمقابلة والجزئية والحلول والسببية والشرطية والمشابهة. (فلايراد الفرس من الكتاب مثلا) قيد للثاني أو الأول أو كليهما.
The relationship is considered general, meaning the necessary connection of the intended meaning to the original meaning in general. Therefore, in majaz there is a transfer from the necessary to the necessary, and kina'a is the opposite. Know that the necessary, as long as it is not intended, does not transfer from it, so they share the transfer from the necessary to the necessary, and it is partial. So it is either similarity like 'lion' for a brave man, or origination like 'hand' for blessing, or manifestation like in 'And the hand of Allah is above their hands' meaning power due to the appearance of effect in it, or adjacency like 'the water skin' for the waterskin, or partiality like 'eye' for a spy, or universality like 'fingers' in 'they put their fingers in their ears', or ['awwaliyya] like 'the sky rained plants', or [kawwiniyya] like 'give orphans their wealth', or [awwaliyya] like 'I see myself pressing wine', or [mahalliyya] like 'ask the village', or [haliyya] like 'in the mercy of Allah', or [aliyya] like 'appoint for me a tongue of truth' meaning mention. Or absolute, or restricted, or included like 'animal' for horse, or specific like 'horse' for animal, or potential like 'intoxicant' for wine, or necessary or necessitating like 'I compensated Zayd in beating him and I beat him so I compensated him', or causal or caused like 'fire' for heat and 'heat' for fire, or relation like 'beating' for the beater and vice versa, or conditional like 'faith' for prayer, or conditioned like its opposite, or indicative or indicated. So the total of relations is twenty-eight. And al-Muhaqqiq al-Taftazani said: The types of valid relations are many, rising to what they mentioned to twenty-five, and the author of al-Tawdih has summarized them in nine: existence, priority, preparedness, opposition, partiality, inherence, causality, conditionality, and similarity. (For example, 'horse' is not intended from 'book') a restriction for the second or the first or both.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage is a detailed classification of the types of relations (alaqat) that govern majaz (metaphorical usage) according to the author. It begins by defining the relationship as a necessary connection between the borrowed meaning and the original meaning in general. Then it lists 28 specific types of relations with examples from Quranic and Arabic usage. The enumeration includes similarity (mushabaha), origination (masdariyya), manifestation (mazhariyya), adjacency (mujawara), part-to-whole (ju'iyya), whole-to-part (kulliyya), and many others such as causal, conditional, and relational. After the list, the author notes that al-Taftazani counted 25 types, while the author of al-Tawdih compressed them into nine categories: existence, priority, preparedness, opposition, partiality, inherence, causality, conditionality, and similarity. The passage ends with a restriction: one cannot take 'horse' from 'book' arbitrarily; the relation must be valid. This classification is crucial for defining valid majaz in balaghah and usul al-fiqh.

Key Terms
العلاقة (al-alaqa): The relationship between the literal meaning and the figurative meaning in majaz, which must be a necessary connection approved by Arabic usage. [Note: In earlier logic works, 'alaqa is not a standard term; it emerges in balaghah and usul al-fiqh discussions of majaz.]
المجاز (al-majaz): Usage of a word in a meaning other than its original, based on a valid relationship (alaqa) with a contextual indicator (qarina). [Note: In usul al-fiqh, majaz is broader than in balaghah; the author notes this distinction later.]

D) Using 'fingers' to mean the whole hand

Key Takeaway
The 28 relations of majaz provide a systematic framework for analyzing figurative language in Arabic, linking literal and intended meanings through valid connections.

When Is a Qarina Required? The Role of the Muharrir
Author defines qarina and explains when it is obligatory for mujib and mustadill.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a qarina (contextual indicator) is required for a majaz or mushtarak based on the role of the muharrir (manifestor) as either a preventer (mani') or a prover (mustadill).

Core Idea
In jadal, whether you need a qarina depends on whether you are explaining to prevent misunderstanding or to prove a point.

This passage introduces a key distinction in the requirement of qarina in dialectical contexts. It defines qarina and then explains that it is not always necessary when clarifying meaning. The condition hinges on the role of the muharrir: if he is merely preventing misunderstanding (mani'), no qarina is needed; but if he uses his clarification as a premise in a proof (mustadill), a qarina is essential. Let's break down the logic.

Guided Path
1
Identify the definiendum
Locate the term being defined in the Arabic: 'القرينة'. Note that the definition follows: 'what reveals the intended meaning not by lexical assignment, whether situational or verbal'.
This definition is broader than the balaghah definition, where qarina is always required for majaz.
2
Understand the definition components
The definition has two key parts: (1) it reveals the intended meaning, (2) not by the word's lexical assignment itself. Qarinas can be situational (haliyya) or verbal (maqaliyya).
Contrast with the word's literal meaning (wad') which is by lexical assignment.
3
Differentiate the two roles of the muharrir
The passage contrasts two roles: if the muharrir is a 'mani'' (preventer) – someone who merely responds to an objection by clarifying his intended meaning – then a qarina is not obligatory. If he is a 'mustadill' (prover) who uses his clarification as a premise in a proof, then a qarina is required for the majaz.
The muharrir may be the original speaker (mu'allal) or someone else, affecting the strength of the qarina.
4
Apply the condition to a sample scenario
Consider: A speaker uses the word 'lion' metaphorically to mean a brave man. If a listener objects and the speaker merely says 'I meant a brave man' (as a preventer), no extra qarina is needed. But if the speaker uses that interpretation as evidence to prove a point (e.g., to argue that bravery is praiseworthy), then he must provide a qarina showing that 'lion' is not literal.
This distinction prevents unnecessary burden on the responder while ensuring rigorous proof when needed.
5
Note the special case of the original speaker
The author states that if the responder is the original speaker himself, his own statement 'my intended meaning is this' is among the strongest preventing qarinas.
This is because the speaker's own clarification carries the highest authority in revealing intent.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وأما القرينة ( وهى ما يفصح عن المراد لا بالوضع حالية كانت أو مقالية ، وبعبارة أخرى لفظية أو معنوية (المانعة عن إرادة) معنى الكلمة (الحقيقة فلا تجب تلك القرينة عليه كما لا تجب القرينة المعينة للمعنى المراد سواء كان في المجاز أو فى المشترك (إذا كان المحرر مانعا أي مجيبا بتحرير المراد سواء كان نفس المعلل أو شخصا غيره. وأما إذا كان مستدلا وجعل تحريره مقدمة من دليل فلابد للجاز من قرينة مانعة عن إرادة الحقيقة ، هذا إذا كان المجيب بالتحرير شخصا غير المعلل يريد الجواب عن طرف المعلل. وأما إذا كان المجيب نفس المعلل، فقوله بأن مرادي هذا من أقوى القرائن المانعة وكذا الحال في القرينة المعينة مطلقا.
And as for the contextual indicator (al-qarina) – and it is that which reveals the intended meaning not by direct lexical assignment, whether it is situational or verbal, in other words verbal or conceptual – that prevents from intending the (literal) meaning of the word, that qarina is not obligatory for him, just as the specifying qarina for the intended meaning is not obligatory, whether in majaz or in a shared term, when the explainer (al-muharrir) is a preventer (mani'), i.e., a responder by clarifying the intended meaning, whether he is the same as the original speaker (al-mu'allal) or someone else. And if he is a prover (mustadill), making his clarification a premise of his proof, then for the majaz there must be a qarina preventing from intending the literal meaning. This is when the responder by clarification is someone other than the original speaker, intending to answer the other party. And if the responder is the same as the original speaker, then his saying 'my intended meaning is this' is among the strongest preventing qarinas, and likewise for the specifying qarina in absolute terms.
Source pages p. 59

Commentary
This passage defines al-qarina (contextual indicator) as that which indicates the intended meaning not by lexical assignment, whether it is situational (haliyya) or verbal (maqaliyya), i.e., linguistic or non-linguistic. The author then explains the condition for its requirement. The key distinction is based on the role of the 'muharrir' (the one who clarifies). If the muharrir is only a 'mani'' (preventer) – that is, someone who merely responds to an objection by clarifying what he meant, without relying on that clarification as a proof – then a qarina is not obligatory for him. This applies whether the muharrir is the original speaker or someone else. However, if the muharrir is a 'mustadill' (prover) who uses his clarification as a premise in a proof, then a qarina is necessary for the majaz to prevent the literal meaning. Furthermore, if the responder is the original speaker himself, his own statement 'I meant this' is considered one of the strongest qarinas. The same rule applies to the specifying qarina (al-mu'ayyina) in all cases. This lesson establishes a nuanced rule for the requirement of qarina depending on the dialogical context.

Key Terms
القرينة
المحرر

Key Terms from Source Passage

D) Only in the case of a shared term (mushtarak)

Key Takeaway
The requirement of qarina depends on whether the explainer is a prover (mustadill) or a mere preventer (mani').

The respondent (a jurist) replies: 'I interpret it as indicating obligation only if there is no qarina showing it is for recommendation.' Is the respondent acting as a mani' or mustadill? Does he need to provide a qarina? Explain using the passage.

Classification of Qarina and Its Role in Majaz
The author classifies qarina and clarifies its necessity for majaz in argumentation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify qarina as certain or probable, determine the type of haqiqa it conflicts with, and reconstruct the dialectical debate about its necessity in tahrir.

Core Idea
The qarina is necessary only for certainty, not for possibility in debate.

This passage from the Wala'diyya commentary is a rich dialectical exchange. It first clarifies that the objector relying on possibility does not need a preventing qarina, then provides a taxonomy of qarina and haqiqa. Understanding this classification is crucial for anyone engaged in tahrir in Islamic legal and theological argumentation.

Guided Path
1
Identify the initial objection about kina'ya
The passage begins with an objection that majaz cannot be generalized to include kina'ya (metonymy) because kina'ya permits its real meaning. Notice how the author responds by distinguishing between genus and individual.
This sets up the debate about when a qarina is required.
2
Recognize the major premise challenge
The objector argues that if a person satisfied with possibility must have a qarina, then without it the qarina would not be a condition—which is false. The author denies the entailment.
This is a classic conditional argument in jadal.
3
Learn the classification of qarina and haqiqa
The author divides qarina into certain (qat'iyya) and probable (muhtamala), and similarly haqiqa. Certain majaz conflicts with certain haqiqa; probable majaz does not conflict with probable haqiqa.
This taxonomy is foundational for determining the validity of metaphorical interpretation.
4
Analyze al-Qazabadi's example
Al-Qazabadi uses the verse 'And hold fast to the rope of Allah' to show that a probable qarina suffices for a probable majaz, even if certainty is absent.
The existence of a probable qarina is enough for metaphorical probability.
5
Understand the footnote on the mu'allil
The footnote distinguishes between the neutral tahrir (who must provide a qarina when proffering evidence) and the mu'allil himself, whose explicit statement of intention can serve as the strongest qarina.
This clarifies the burden of proof in different roles.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
بجرادة المعنى الكنوى لأنه إنما يصار إليه لعدم صحة معناه الحقيق والسكناية يصح إرادة معناها الحقيقي فكيف يتصور التحرير فلا يصح تعميم المجاز للسكناية . قلت الكناية من حيث إنها كناية يجوز فيها إرادة لمعنى الحقيق لكنها قد يمتنع الخصوص مادة كما في قوله تعالى وليس كمثله شيء) وقوله الرحمن على العرش استوى ، ولذا قبل القرينة المانعة منافية لجنس الكناية لا لكل فرد منها لأن المانع يكفيه الجواز) عنى المانع المحرر يكفيه الجواز لأن التحرير حينئذ يكون سندا لمنعه والسنديكفي جواز وقوعه فى الاستناد فلا يجيب وعلى المانع إثبات وقوعه وإن كان في صورة الجزم وكل من يكفيه الجواز لا يجب عليه القرينة المانعة فلا يجب عليه القرينة المانعة إذا كان مانعا ، ولما ورد على كبرى هذا الدليل بأن كل من يكفيه الجواز يجب عليه القرينة وإلا أى وإن لم يجب عليه القرينة لم تكن القرينة شرطا فى المجاز والنالى باطله فكذا المقدم أجاب بمنع الملازمة بقوله ( والقرينة المانعة إنما تشترط للقطع بالمعنى المجازى لا تجويزه) وحاصله المنع بالترديد فافهم : اعلم أن القرينة على قسمين قرينة قطعية وقرينة محتملة فان كان المجاز قطعيا فلابد له من قرينة قطعية، وإن كان محتملا فلابد له من قرينة محتملة. والحقيقة أيضا على قسمين حقيقة قطعية وحقيقة محتملة والمجاز القطعى مناف للحقيقة القطعية والمجاز المحتمل لا ينا في الحقيقة المحتملة وإن كان منافيا للحقيقة القطعية . قال العلامة القازابادي نفعنا الله بركاته في شرح رسالة الاستعارة في قوله ويحتمل الوجهين قوله تعالى واعتصموا بحبل الله وههنا بحث وهو أنه إن وجد قرينة مانعة عن إراده معناه فلا مجال للوجه الأول وإن لم توجد فلا مجال للوجه الثاني. ويمكن أن يجاب عنه بأن وجود القرينة محتمل ههنا لاقطعى وهذا القدر يكفى فى احتمال المجاز، والمراد ههنا احتمال المجاز لا قطعه ، ( لأن المانع يكفيه الجواز ) أى جواز مستنده ( والقرينة المانعة إنما يشترك ) وجودها والقطع بها القطع بالمعنى المجازى لا لتجويزه) مع تجويز المعنى الحقيقى هكذا استفيد من التقرير لكن هنا نظر لأن تجويز المعنى الحقيقى مع تجويز المعنى المجازى بدون القرينة المانعة لا يمكن إلا فى بعض الكناية وقد عرفت أن ذلك البعض لا يتصور التحرير بمعناه فكيف يتصور الاستناد به قلم أن التحرير بارادة المجاز لا يصح بدون القطع بالعلاقة للمصححة والقرينة المانعة . فان قلت يقول بهما قلت ظهور المعنى الحقيقي قائم مقام من دليل مبطل جواز إرادة المعنى المجازى فلا يجوز الاستناد والاستدلال به إلا بعد ذلك الدليل بالقطع بهما فلا تغفل. إن قلت تجويز المعني الحقيقى مع تجويز المعنى المجازى على طريق المجاز شائع فى الكتاب والسنة بدون القرينة الصارفة إذ لو كانت هنا لما جاز معناه الحقيقى. قلت بلوغهما غاية القصوي في البلاغة والفصاحة قرينة صارفة من الحقيقة إلى المجاز لكونه أبلغ منها لكن لما عارضه ظهورها كان لكل منها جهة هو موليه فالقرينة الصارفة موجودة هنا قطعا لكنه مع المعارض بخلاف كلام المناظر من مع أن ذلك المجاز لا يتصور التحرير به أيضا فلا يستند به تأمل . قال في الحاشية: وأما إذا كان المحر و مستدلا وجعل تحرير مقدمة من دليله فلابد المجاز من بيان قرينة مانعة عن إرادة الحقيقة هذا إذا كان المجيب بالتحرير شخصا غير المعلل يريد الجواب عن طرف المعلل . وأما إذا كان المجيب هو المعلل فقوله بأن مرادى هذا من أقوى القرينة المانعة انتهى وفيه نظر ، إذ قول المعلل بعد اعتراض السائل بأن مرادى هذا بدون ميان قرينة حالية أو مقالية إحجام لأنه نصب قرينة بالمراد، واعتراف بفساد المعترض عليه من الكلام مع أن جعله قرينة لم صرف عن الكلام . وأما القرينة المعينة فلا تجب إلا إذا كان المحرر مستدلا : وكذا عدم وجود القرينة محتمل ههنا لاقطمي ويكفى هذا فى احتمال الحقيقة، والمراد هنا هو احتمال الحقيقة انتهى وما يقال من أن الحقيقة إذا أمكنت لا يصار إلى المجاز ، فالمراد به الحقيقة القطعية لا المحتملة ويؤيده أن كتب التفاسير والأحاديث مشحونة بالحمل على المعاني المجازية مع إمكان لمعانى الحقيقية فلايرد عليه ماقيل ، لكن هنا نظر لأن تجويز المعنى الحقيقى مع تجويز المعنى المجازى بدون الفرينة المانعة لا يمكن إلا في بعض الكناية ، وقد عرفت أن ذلك لا يتصور التحرير بمعناه فكيف يتصور الاستناد به . فعلم أن التحرير بارادة المجاز لا يصح بدون القطع بالعلاقة المصححة والفرينة المانعة
By the word of metonymy, because it is only resorted to due to the invalidity of its real meaning, while metonymy allows intending its real meaning, so how can tahrir be conceived? Thus, it is not correct to generalize majaz to metonymy. I said: Metonymy, insofar as it is metonymy, it is permissible to intend the real meaning in it, but it may be prevented by particular matter, as in His saying: 'There is nothing like Him' and His saying: 'The Most Merciful established Himself over the Throne.' Therefore, it is accepted that the preventing qarina conflicts with the genus of metonymy, not with every individual of it, because the objector is satisfied with possibility—meaning the objector who performs tahrir is satisfied with possibility, because tahrir then becomes a support for his objection, and the support only requires the possibility of its occurrence in reliance, so he does not have to respond, and the objector must prove its occurrence even if in the form of certainty, and whoever is satisfied with possibility is not obligated to provide a preventing qarina; thus, he is not obligated to provide a preventing qarina when he is an objector. And when it was objected to the major premise of this proof that whoever is satisfied with possibility must have the qarina—otherwise, if he is not required to have the qarina, then the qarina is not a condition for majaz, and the consequent is false, so likewise the antecedent—he answered by denying the entailment with his saying: 'The preventing qarina is only required for certainty of the metaphorical meaning, not for its possibility.' In summary, it is a denial by disjunction, so understand. Know that qarina is of two kinds: certain qarina and probable qarina. If the majaz is certain, it must have a certain qarina; and if it is probable, it must have a probable qarina. Haqiqa also is of two kinds: certain haqiqa and probable haqiqa. Certain majaz contradicts certain haqiqa, and probable majaz does not contradict probable haqiqa, though it contradicts certain haqiqa. Al-'Allamah al-Qazabadi (may Allah benefit us with his blessings) said in his commentary on the Treatise of Metaphor in his statement 'and it admits both aspects'—His saying: 'And hold fast to the rope of Allah'—and here there is a discussion: if there is a preventing qarina from intending its meaning, then there is no room for the first aspect; and if not, then there is no room for the second aspect. It can be answered that the existence of the qarina here is probable, not certain, and this amount suffices for the probability of majaz; the intended meaning here is the probability of majaz, not its certainty. (Because the objector is satisfied with possibility), i.e., the possibility of his support. (And the preventing qarina is only required) for certainty of the metaphorical meaning, not for its possibility, along with the possibility of the real meaning. This is understood from the exposition, but here there is a consideration: because the possibility of the real meaning along with the possibility of the metaphorical meaning without the preventing qarina is only possible in some metonymy, and you have known that that part cannot be conceived with tahrir in its meaning, so how can reliance be conceived upon it? It is clear that tahrir with intent of majaz is not valid without certainty of the justifying relationship and the preventing qarina. If you say: He asserts both, I say: The apparentness of the real meaning stands in the place of a proof invalidating the permissibility of intending the metaphorical meaning, so it is not permissible to rely and infer by it except after that proof with certainty of both, so do not be heedless. If you say: The permissibility of the real meaning along with the permissibility of the metaphorical meaning in the way of majaz is widespread in the Book and Sunnah without the diverting qarina, because if it were present, then its real meaning would not be permissible. I say: Their reaching the utmost degree in eloquence and rhetoric is a diverting qarina from haqiqa to majaz, because it is more eloquent than it, but when its apparentness opposes it, each has a direction it faces. So the diverting qarina is certainly present here, but with opposition, unlike the speech of the disputant, with that majaz cannot be conceived with tahrir either, so it is not relied upon—reflect. He said in the footnote: As for when the one performing tahrir is a proponent of evidence, and he makes his tahrir a premise of his proof, then he must indicate a preventing qarina for intending the real meaning. This applies when the respondent with tahrir is a person other than the one providing the cause, intending to answer the side of the one providing the cause. As for when the respondent is the one providing the cause himself, his statement that 'my intention is this' is among the strongest preventing qarinas. End quote. There is consideration: since the statement of the one providing the cause after the objection of the questioner that 'my intention is this' without any contextual or verbal qarina is a retreat because he sets up the qarina by intention, and an admission of corruption of the object of objection from the speech, while making it a qarina does not divert from the speech. As for the specifying qarina, it is only obligatory if the one performing tahrir is a proponent of evidence. And likewise, the absence of the qarina is probable here, not certain, and this suffices for probability of haqiqa, and the intended meaning here is probability of haqiqa. End quote. And what is said that if haqiqa is possible, majaz is not resorted to—what is meant by it is certain haqiqa, not probable haqiqa, and this is supported by the fact that books of exegesis and hadith are filled with carrying meanings metaphorically despite the possibility of real meanings, so what is said does not refute it. But here there is a consideration: because the possibility of the real meaning along with the possibility of the metaphorical meaning without the preventing qarina is only possible in some metonymy, and you have known that that cannot be conceived with tahrir in its meaning, so how can reliance be conceived upon it? Thus, it is known that tahrir with intent of majaz is not valid without certainty of the justifying relationship and the preventing qarina.
Source pages p. 60

Commentary
This passage is a dialectical discussion on the role of the qarina (contextual indicator) in metaphorical usage (majaz) within logical argumentation (tahrir). The author begins by addressing an objection concerning the generalization of majaz to include metonymy (kina'ya), noting that metonymy permits the real meaning and thus cannot be treated as pure majaz. He then responds to an objection: if a disputant is satisfied with mere possibility (juwaz), he is not obligated to provide a qarina, because the qarina is only necessary for certainty, not possibility. The author clarifies that qarina is divided into two types: certain (qat'iyya) and probable (muhtamala), and similarly haqiqa (literal meaning) is of two types. Certain majaz conflicts with certain haqiqa, but probable majaz does not conflict with probable haqiqa. He cites al-Qazabadi's example from the Qur'an 'And hold fast to the rope of Allah' to illustrate that when a qarina is probable, it suffices for probability of majaz. The discussion continues with further objections and answers, emphasizing that tahrir using majaz requires both a justifying relationship (al-'ilaqa al-musahhiha) and a preventing qarina (al-qarina al-mani'a). The passage also examines a footnote distinguishing between the case where the one performing tahrir is a neutral party and when he is the one providing the cause (mu'allil). In the latter case, his explicit statement of intention can serve as the strongest qarina. Ultimately, the author concludes that without certainty of the justifying relationship and the preventing qarina, tahrir with majaz is invalid.

المجاز
الحقيقة
القطعي

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the objector satisfied with possibility not need to provide a preventing qarina?
A) Because the qarina is only needed for certainty of metaphorical meaning, not possibility.
B) Because the objector is always certain.
C) Because majaz is always possible without qarina.
D) Because kina'ya never requires qarina.

Key Takeaway
The qarina is necessary only for certainty in metaphorical interpretation; for probable interpretation, a probable qarina suffices, and in dialectical contexts, the objector may rely on mere possibility.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. In the debate about whether the verse 'There is nothing like Him' (Qur'an 42:11) can be interpreted metaphorically, identify which type of qarina (certain or probable) is present and what type of haqiqa it conflicts with.
?
2. Reconstruct the objection-response pattern: Take the position of the objector (mu'tarid) and respond using the principle that the qarina is only required for certainty, not for possibility.

The Proposition and Its Seven Names: A Foundational Definition
Author defines the proposition and lists its seven names by perspective.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the proposition (qadiyya) and recall its seven names based on different perspectives, as enumerated by al-Taftazani.

Core Idea
One essence, multiple names: the proposition is defined by its truth-aptness, but named by its role.

The author opens the third chapter by defining the proposition as a truth-apt complete compound. He then explains how the term 'assent' relates to it, and quotes al-Taftazani's enumeration of seven names for the same entity. This passage sets the terminological foundation for the rest of the chapter on dialectical debate about assent.

Guided Path
1
Locate the definition of the proposition
Find the Arabic phrase 'المركب التام الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب' (the complete compound that admits truth and falsehood). This is the core definition of qadiyya.
2
Analyze the metonymic use of 'tasdiq'
The author gives four explanations for why 'assent' (tasdiq) is applied to the proposition. Notice that in standard logic, tasdiq is an act of the mind, not the proposition itself.
This usage is specific to this dialectical context.
3
List the seven names from al-Taftazani
Extract each name (qadiyya, khabar, ikhbar, muqaddima, matlub, natija, mas'ala) and their associated perspectives as given in the quote.
The perspective (i'tibar) determines the name, not a change in essence.
4
Apply the principle of unified essence
Understand the statement 'الذات واحدة واختلاف العبارات باختلاف الاعتبارات' (the essence is one, expressions differ by considerations). This is the key takeaway.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الباب الثالث من الأبواب الثلاثة في بيان المناظرة الجارية في التصديق، وهو القضية. والقضية هي المركب التام الذي يحتمل الصدق والكذب. وإطلاق التصديق على القضية: إما حقيقة عرفية أو من قبيل إطلاق اسم العلم بالجزء على الكل على مذهب الحكماء أو من قبيل إطلاق اسم العلم على المعلوم على مذهب الإمام أو جعل التصديق بمعنى المصدق به فأطلق عليها. قال التفتازاني في التلويح: اعلم أن المركب المحتمل للصدق والكذب يسمى من حيث اشتماله على الحكم قضية، ومن حيث احتماله للصدق والكذب خبرا، ومن حيث إفادته الحكم إخبارا، ومن حيث كونه جزءا من الدليل مقدمة، ومن حيث يطلب بالدليل مطلوبا، ومن حيث يحصل من الدليل نتيجة، ومن حيث يقع في العلم ويسأل عنه مسألة. فالذات واحدة واختلاف العبارات باختلاف الاعتبارات.
The third chapter of the three chapters concerning the dialectical debate on assent, and it is the proposition. The proposition is the complete compound that admits truth and falsehood. The application of 'assent' to the proposition is either a customary reality, or of the type of applying the name of knowledge of the part to the whole according to the philosophers, or of the type of applying the name of knowledge to the known according to the Imam, or making 'assent' in the sense of 'that which is assented to', so it is applied to it. Al-Taftazani said in al-Talwih: Know that the compound which admits truth and falsehood is called, from the perspective of its containing a judgment, a proposition; from the perspective of its admitting truth and falsehood, a report; from the perspective of its conveying a judgment, an informing; from the perspective of its being part of a proof, a premise; from the perspective of its being sought by proof, a sought; from the perspective of its being obtained from proof, a result; and from the perspective of its occurring in knowledge and being asked about, a question. The essence is one, and the variation of expressions is by variation of considerations.
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage opens the third chapter of the work, which deals with dialectical debate specifically concerning assent (tasdiq). The author first clarifies that by 'assent' he means the proposition (qadiyya). He then defines the proposition as a complete compound that is truth-apt. He explains several ways in which 'assent' is applied to the proposition: either as a customary usage, or as a part-whole name transfer, or as knowledge-name transfer, or by regarding 'assent' as 'that which is assented to'. Then he quotes al-Taftazani from al-Talwih, who lists seven names for the same entity based on different considerations: qadiyya (for containing a judgment), khabar (for admitting truth/falsehood), ikhbar (for conveying a judgment), muqaddima (as part of a proof), matlub (as what is sought by proof), natija (as the result of proof), and mas'ala (as a question in knowledge). The key point is that the underlying essence is one; only the perspectives differ. This is a foundational definitional passage that sets the terminology for the chapter.

تصديق
مركب تام
خبر
إخبار
مقدمة
مطلوب
نتيجة
مسألة

A) قضية
B) خبر
C) مقدمة
D) نتيجة

Key Takeaway
The proposition is a truth-apt complete compound; its various names (qadiyya, khabar, muqaddima, etc.) reflect different roles and perspectives, not different entities.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every intoxicant is forbidden' and list its seven names according to al-Taftazani's perspectives (e.g., from the perspective of containing a judgment, it is a qadiyya; from being part of a proof, it is a muqaddima; etc.).
?

Tasdiq as Da'wa and the Mu'allil
Defines da'wa and mu'allil based on utterance.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify when a tasdiq becomes a da'wa and who is obligated to provide a cause for it.

Core Idea
Every uttered claim demands a cause; ignorance is not an excuse.

In dialectic, not every statement is a claim. Only when a tasdiq is uttered by someone does it become a da'wa that the speaker must be ready to defend. This lesson explains the transformation of assent into a claim and the role of the mu'allil, the one who must provide justification.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act of utterance
Note that the passage explicitly says: if someone says the assent explicitly or implicitly, it becomes da'wa. So look for the condition of being uttered.
2
Contrast with performatives (insha')
The passage states that performatives are not tasdiq and are not called da'wa. Understand that da'wa is specifically about claims that can be true or false, not about creating reality through speech.
3
Define the speaker's obligation
The speaker of a da'wa is called mu'allil, meaning his very role is to provide a cause (ta'lil). Even if he currently has no cause, it is his right and duty to produce one.
This sets the burden of proof in debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
اعلم أن التصديق حقيقة أو معنى، ولهذا أظهر في موضع الإضمار. إذا قاله أحد صريحا أو ضمنا من عند نفسه يقال له ذلك التصديق الدعوى والمدعى، صريحة أو ضمنية، نظرية أو بديهية خفية أو جلية. وإنما سمي بهما لأن القائل ادعاه والتزمه، فالإنشاء ليس بتصديق لأنه إذا قاله أحد لا يقال له الدعوى والمدعى. وقائله المعلل، أي الذي من شأنه التعليل، سواء كان له تعليل بالفعل أو لا، لأن من حقه التعليل عليه، أي على التصديق أو على المدعى. اعلم أن التعليل تبيين علة الشيء، كالاستدلال. والتعليل مختص بالدليل اللمي، وهو تبيين علة الشيء لحصول التصديق بالمطلوب، كما إذا قلنا هذا محموم لأنه متعفن الأخلاط وكل متعفن الأخلاط محموم. والاستدلال بالعكس، وهو تبيين معلول الشيء لحصول التصديق بالمطلوب، كما إذا قلنا هذا متعفن الأخلاط لأنه محموم وكل محموم متعفن الأخلاط، وهو مختص بالدليل الإنّي.
Know that assent, in reality or in meaning, and for this it is made manifest in the place of implicit reference. If someone says it explicitly or implicitly from himself, that assent is called the claim and the claimed, explicit or implicit, theoretical or self-evident hidden or clear. It is only called by these two because the speaker claims it and commits to it, so a performative utterance is not an assent because if someone says it, it is not called claim and claimed. And its speaker is the mu'allil, i.e., the one who is supposed to provide the cause (ta'lil), whether he actually has a cause or not, because it is his right to provide the cause for it, i.e., for the assent or for the claimed. Know that ta'lil is the explanation of the cause of a thing, like istidlal. And ta'lil is specific to the demonstrative proof of 'why' (dalil limmi), which is explaining the cause of a thing to obtain assent to the sought, as when we say: 'This person is feverish because his humors are putrified, and every putrified-humors person is feverish.' And istidlal is the opposite: it is explaining the effect of a thing to obtain assent to the sought, as when we say: 'This person is putrified of humors because he is feverish, and every feverish person is putrified of humors,' and it is specific to the demonstrative proof of 'that' (dalil inni).
Source pages p. 61

Commentary
This passage defines the key terms of dialectical debate. It begins by stating that assent (tasdiq), when uttered explicitly or implicitly, is called 'da'wa' (claim) and 'mudda'a' (the claimed). This applies whether the claim is explicit or implicit, theoretical or self-evident (hidden or clear). The reason for this naming is that the speaker claims and commits to it; performatives (insha') are not included. The speaker of this claim is called 'mu'allil' (one who provides a cause), because it is his right to provide a justification (ta'lil) for the assent or for the claimed. The passage then distinguishes two types of justification: ta'lil and istidlal. Ta'lil (causal explanation) is explaining the cause of a thing to establish the sought conclusion; it is associated with the 'limmi' proof (demonstration of the reason why). The example given: 'This person is feverish because his humors are putrified, and everything with putrified humors is feverish.' Here the cause (putrified humors) is stated to prove the effect (fever). Istidlal (inference from effect) is the opposite: it explains the effect to arrive at the cause; it is associated with the 'inni' proof (demonstration of the that). Example: 'This person's humors are putrified because he is feverish, and every feverish person has putrified humors.' This distinction is fundamental in logic and dialectic, showing the direction of explanation.

Key Terms
دعوى
معلل
تعليل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT considered a da'wa according to the passage?
A) An explicit assent such as 'The world is created'
B) An implicit assent when a speaker says 'The world is created' implicitly
C) A performative utterance such as 'You are divorced'
D) A theoretical claim like 'Every created thing has a creator'

Key Takeaway
A tasdiq becomes a da'wa only when uttered, and the speaker (mu'allil) bears the burden of providing a cause.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
1. Identify the da'wa in the following statement: 'The Qur'an is uncreated' uttered by a theologian. Is it a da'wa? Who is the mu'allil?
?
2. Explain why a performative utterance like 'I divorce you' is not considered a da'wa in this context.
